PREFACE OF THE MAYOR
Dear Residents of İstanbul,
Since the commencement of our tenure, we continue to work and produce with the goal of a fair, green and creative İstanbul, and to offer a better city life to İstanbul and its valuable residents. İstanbul is experiencing the era which stands as an unprecedented period of extensive investments in metro systems and infrastructural advancements within the city's history. 
We have undersigned the firsts in the history of IMM such as İstanbul Halk Dairy, Yuvamız İstanbul nurseries, student dormitories, 
Anne Card, Regional Employment Offices, 
City Restaurants, life centers and life valleys. 
It is with great satisfaction that we acknowledge the successful collaboration with local farmers and producers, facilitated by our support, ensuring efficient production unburdened by financial constraints. 
Employing a participatory, transparent, people-oriented approach that values collective visdom, we have conceptualized and executed projects that safeguard the collective interests of 16 million İstanbul residents while preparing İstanbul for the future. We continued the 150 Projects in 150 
Days, which started with the opening of the 
Basilica Cistern on July 22, 2022, with the 
300 Projects in 300 Days marathon. This marathon has accelerated our investments, leading many works to İstanbul and laid the foundations of many projects. 
The foundation of the İçerenköy Sports Facility marked the initiation of our "New 
Investments, 
New 
Breakthroughs" marathon, which we eagerly anticipate.
Our efforts also extend to safeguarding İstanbul against environmental risks. The climate crisis threatens life around the world. The repercussions of this crisis, such as rising temperatures, forest fires, drought risks, and challenges pertaining to water and food security, necessitate a coordinated global response. Waste of no time, IMM has initiated concerted actions to address this exigent issue. We remain actively engaged in monitoring both local and global initiatives aimed at tackling this challenge.
Upon assuming office, we established the İstanbul Planning Agency to embark on the formulation of the Vision 2050 Strategy 
Document, a comprehensive roadmap for İstanbul's multifaceted future. The Vision 
2050 Strategy Document was created as a result of data-based analysis with the ideas, efforts and contributions of thousands of 
Sustainable Energy and Climate Action Plan Of İstanbul

[Page 18]
İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality people. Throughout its development, the document has benefitted not only from our internal teams but also from the insights of nearly 
2.000 representatives spanning academia, public entities, civil society organizations, private sector institutions, and almost 25 thousand citizens. One of the main themes of this document was the environment and the city adapting to the changing climate.
Under the umbrella of the İstanbul 
Metropolitan Municipality, we started to produce green energy with the solar energy systems we installed in IMM premises. We expanded green spaces in our city, we protected İstanbul's sea and water beds. 
We commenced environmental facilities, especially the 
Kemerburgaz 
Waste 
Incineration and Energy Production Facility, the largest in Europe, and the Silivri Seymen 
Landfill Gas Power Generation Facility. Our aspirations extend towards making İstanbul a carbon-neutral and climate-resilient global city by 2050. We will achieve this goal by decarbonizing the transportation system and improving the integration of public modes and fostering synergy with other transit options.
Presently, we possess the İstanbul 
Sustainable Energy and Climate Action Plan for 2023. In alignment with the İstanbul 
Metropolitan 
Municipality's resolute commitment to combatting climate change, 
IMM focuses on green energy transformation in buildings, equipment, urban transportation systems and recycling of waste. We anticipate this endeavor will be beneficial and auspicious for our city and our fellow citizens. I extend my heartfelt gratitude to all contributors who have been instrumental in shaping this report, on behalf of 16 million İstanbulites.
This year we celebrate the 
100th anniversary of our Republic. As we step into the second century of our Republic's existence, we remain devoted to work with the same faith and enthusiasm, adding value to İstanbul with the projects we will implement in the light of practical wisdom and scientific insights.
With profound regard and utmost esteem,
Mayor of İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality

[Page 20]
EXECUTIVE SUMMARY municipal affairs of the province of İstanbul. 35% (1869 km²) of the total land area of İstanbul, which is within the jurisdiction of the İstanbul 
Metropolitan Municipality, is located on the Anatolian Side and 65% (3474 km²) is on the European side.1
Since İstanbul is a city that is acutely aware of the vulnerability created by climate change and being a signatory of 
Global Covenant of Mayors (GCoM), 
IMM is committed to reduce GHG emissions 40% by 2030 from 2019 levels and prepare and implement Sustainable 
Energy and 
Climate 
Action 
Plan (SECAP) in this respect. SECAP defines the activities and measures set up to achieve this target. For Climate Change mitigation, the main target sectors are buildings, equipment/facilities, urban transport, and waste.
As part of İstanbul 
Metropolitan 
Municipality's membership in the C40 
Cities 
Climate 
Leadership 
Group, 
Mayor Mr. Ekrem İmamoğlu signed the 
Deadline 2020 Commitment, accepting the goal of making İstanbul a carbon-neutral and resilient city by 
2050. In line with this new target, the İstanbul is the most populous city in 
Türkiye and Europe. It is located on both sides of the Bosphorus, the strait that connects the Black Sea to the Sea of 
Marmara. As of 2019, the population of İstanbul is 15.519.267 and it is estimated to be just over 17 million by 2030.
İstanbul the hub for the country's commercial, historical, and cultural activity. The city is a major hub for trade and commerce, banking and finance, manufacturing, media and entertainment, and tourism. It is home to many of the country’s largest banks, as well as a large number of foreign banks. 
As stated in the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC) Assessment 
Reports, 
Türkiye is in the 
Eastern 
Mediterranean Basin, which is one of the regions sensitive to climate change. 
According to the annual average temperature projections presented in the 
IPCC 5th Assessment Report, increases are predicted throughout Türkiye in all scenarios and throughout the entire period (2016 - 2100). İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality (IMM) is the public institution responsible for İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality

[Page 21]
calculations 
GHG emissions by sectors in the city level show that total 
GHG emissions of İstanbul city comprise about 50,89 MtCO2e. Stationary energy sector emissions comprise 63% of all emissions in İstanbul city. The majority of stationary energy sector emissions come from the residential buildings (44,17%). The transportation sector is responsible for 28,3% of total emissions, dominated by on-road transportation (99%). The waste and waste water sector, which is controlled and managed by IMM through companies İSTAÇ and İSKİ, represents the remaining 8,8% of emissions in İstanbul, with the vast majority (89%) of these coming from solid waste disposal. Analysis shows that İstanbul’s municipality insititutional emissions (1,54 MtCO2e) comprise about 
3% of total citywide emissions. Main source of corporate emissions is commercial and institutional buildings and facilities of 
IMM affiliated companies and subsidiaries (54,13%).
As a summary of the works undertaken for risk and vulnerability assessment and determination of adaptation actions, a series of adaptation actions were prioritized for the identified vulnerabilities of the city of İstanbul. All of the problems arising from the vulnerability assessments may not be immediately resolved throughout İstanbul due to technical and budgetary revision process of the CCAP (Climate 
Change Action Plan), which was initially prepared in 2018, commenced, and the Revised İstanbul Climate 
Change Action Plan was prepared and published in 20212. This CCAP outlines the necessary steps for the entire city to take in order to combat climate change. The SECAP presented herein is designed to complement the CCAP and does not aim to reiterate the actions already identified for the city, but rather focus on the measures related to the 
IMM buildings, transport, facilities, and its affiliates/companies and intensify the sustainable energy and climate actions. The GHG inventory, risk and vulnerability assessment, targets and goals defined are relevant to the local conditions and reflect the specific activities, capacity and regulatory context of IMM.
IMM has created the institutional GHG inventory as an energy consumption inventoryresulting from all activities of the municipality. The institutional GHG emission inventory also includes municipal administrative buildings and activities, as well as the activities of subsidiaries/affiliates. 
The 
Baseline 
Emission 
Inventory (BEI) was developed for baseline year 2019 by calculating CO2e emissions using an activity-based approach. 
Overall 
Sustainable Energy and Climate Action Plan of İstanbul https://cevre.ibb.istanbul/wp-content/uploads/2022/01/ist_iklim_degisikligi_eylem_plani.pdf

[Page 22]
modernizing the largest and most energy-intensive buildings. 19 buildings have been identified and have undergone energy audits and are ready to start their modernization projects. 
The remaining buildings of the municipality will be grouped according to their energy consumption in order to prioritize their modernization, starting with the most energy-intensive buildings. A program, consisting of installing solar PV plants and using alternative energy sources to generate around 50% of the municipality's current electricity demand by 2030, is also planned.
IMM had 28 subsidiaries and 2 affiliated institutions under its control in 
2019.
constraints. Therefore, the climate extremes, system vulnerability and the exposure of İstanbul, as explained in İstanbul 
Climate 
Change 
Action 
Report3 were studied to determine the city vulnerability maps. 
The identification of the regions through these maps, where climate change impacts are concentrated, pointed out vulnerability hotspots. Based on these hotspots, the potentially applicable adaptation actions were identified, which were then narrowed down to the list of adaptation actions as per city's specificities.4
Through 
SECAP, energy efficiency measures are targeted at the sectors with the greatest potential for energy savings, such as the building, industry, transport, and waste sectors. IMM is responsible for public transportation, waste management, water supply, sewerage system and treatment, recreational areas, road paving, urban and zone planning. IMM is not involved in the production, transmission, distribution of electricity, or public street lighting for the city. However, it does operate several energy production plants, such as solar and waste-to-energy plants, to generate electricity for its own use.

Main beneﬁts of the proposed mitigation actions in İstanbul 
Lower costs
Reduced energy consumption and carbon emissions
Enhanced quality of life
Increased regional renewable energy
Energy-efﬁcient transportation İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality

[Page 33]
Reduced energy consumption and carbon emissions
Improving energy efficiency in buildings, ensuring lower energy consumption and therefore lower carbon emissions, will helpto mitigate climate change and improve air quality in İstanbul.
2. 
Lower costs
Implementing energy efficiency measures can help reduce natural gas and electricity costs, as well as maintenance costs for buildings, ultimately leading to lower overall expenses for citizens in İstanbul.
3. 
Enhanced quality of life
By reducing energy and carbon emissions, improving air quality both indoors and outdoors, and increasing energy efficiency, the quality of life for İstanbul citizens could be significantly enhanced. 
By reducing congestion and travel times, energy-efficient transportation could improve traffic flow and reduce the overall stress and frustration associated with transportation in İstanbul. 
By improving waste management practices and increasing waste recovery rates contributes to the development of the circular economy and creates cleaner city and environment. It is essential to decrease the rate of water losses and water usage and to improve waste water recovery and reuse to ensure water resources for all citizens.
4. 
Increased regional renewable energy
As renewable energy production increases, fossil fuel consumption decreases, digitalization in electrification and energy is implemented, and micro smart grids are established, both energy supply security and more suitable energy will be achieved. 
This will result in a more sustainable energy production and consumption system.
5. 
Energy-efficient transportation
Energy efficiency measures could also lead to lower fuel and maintenance costs for transport operators and individuals, helping to reduce the overall financial burden of transportation in İstanbul. 
Overall, increased energy efficiency would be a beneficial move providing significant economic, social, and environmental benefits, making it a worthwhile investment for the city and its citizens.

1.1. 
INTRODUCTION TO SECAP AND 
EU4 ENERGY TRANSITION PROJECT
The Covenant of Mayors (CoM) is an EU based initiative, which began in 2008. 
The CoM later evolved in 2015 into the 
Covenant of Mayors for Climate and 
Energy, stepping up the mitigation target (from 20 % of CO2 emissions reduction by 2020 to the 40 % by 2030) and integrating two more pillars in addition to mitigation: the adaptation and access to energy.
In 2017, the CoM developed into a global initiative, the Global Covenant of 
Mayors (GCoM), bringing together the 
EU Covenant of Mayors and the 
Compact of Mayors. 
The Global Covenant of Mayors is now an international alliance of cities and local authorities with a shared long-term vision of promoting and supporting voluntary action to combat climate change and move to a low-emission resilient society.
Local authorities who have signed the 
EU CoM and GCoM have committed to submit a Sustainable Energy and İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality

The city is home to the third oldest subway in the world, the 573 meter long tunnel built in 1875, and the 23rd longest metro line in the world.
The city is a major center for finance and commerce, and it is a major port for goods coming in and out of the country. İstanbul is also a major center for tourism, and it is home to a wide variety of attractions, from historical sites to modern shopping malls. The city is a major hub for trade and commerce, banking and finance, manufacturing, media and entertainment, and tourism. 
It is home to many of the country’s largest banks, as well as a large number of foreign banks. The city is also home to a number of stock exchanges, including the İstanbul Stock Exchange, the İstanbul Commodity Exchange, and the İstanbul Derivatives Exchange.

1.2. 
INTRODUCTION TO İSTANBUL
Once renowned as the 'capital of capitals', İstanbul is a city of immense historical and cultural significance. 
Situated between the 
Black and 
Marmara 
Seas, as well as the 
Bosphorus, İstanbul is one of the few cities in the world surrounded by water. 
This strategic geographical location has made it a bridge between Asia and 
Europe for centuries, allowing for the exchange of ideas, cultures, and traditions. İstanbul has been home to many civilizations, including the Roman, 
Latin, Eastern Roman, and Ottoman 
Empires, making it one of the oldest cities in the world. This rich history has endowed İstanbul with a unique cultural

Türkiye and the world living in the city. İstanbul is home to a wide variety of residential areas. The city is divided into 
39 districts, each with its own unique character and attractions. The most popular residential areas in İstanbul are located in the European side of the city. 
The affluent districts of Beşiktaş, 
Nişantaşı, and Etiler are some of the most sought-after areas in the city. The 
Asian side of İstanbul is home to some of the city’s oldest neighborhoods. 
Kadıköy is a popular residential area with a vibrant atmosphere and plenty of restaurants and cafes. Other popular areas include Üsküdar, Beylerbeyi, and Ümraniye. The city also has a number of areas which are popular with families and offer a more relaxed atmosphere than the city center.
İstanbul is a city with a breathtaking beauty, a vibrant culture, and a long and rich history. It is a city that will surely never cease to fascinate its residents.

1.2.2. Climate İstanbul is in a region where the transition from a Mediterranean climate with hot and dry summers and mild and rainy winters to a Black Sea climate with hot summers and mild winters but rainy in all seasons takes place. While the southern parts of the province, where the settlement is denser, have characteristics closer to the 
Mediterranean climate, the climate characteristics evolve towards the Black Sea climate as one goes to the north8. Because of these transitions, İstanbul's summers are hot and humid; winters are cold, rainy, and sometimes snowy. The annual average temperature of İstanbul is 14,5°C. As can be seen in 
Table 1, while the average temperature is around 6°C in January, the coldest month, it rises to around 24°C in 
August, the hottest month. In İstanbul, the highest temperature was measured as 41,5°C on 13.07.2000, and the lowest temperature was measured as -16,1°C on 09.02.192910.
İstanbul is a province with high precipitation variability in the north-south direction. While the total annual precipitation is around 650 mm in the south, it occasionally exceeds 
1,000 mm in the middle parts. It decreases again towards the Black Sea coast and decreases to around 850 mm11. The annual average rainfall in İstanbul has been determined as 677,2 mm. The difference in precipitation between the driest and wettest months of the year is approximately 83 mm. The highest total precipitation amount in İstanbul was determined as 136,1 mm on 
10.09.198112. 
As stated in the Intergovernmental 
Panel on 
Climate 
Change (IPCC) 
Assessment Reports, Türkiye is in the 
Eastern Mediterranean Basin, which is one of the regions sensitive to climate change. In the 6th Assessment Report of the IPCC, it is predicted that if the average temperature increase is 1.5 °C, the annual average temperature change in Türkiye will increase above the global average temperature change.
Climate change in İstanbul is expected to occur primarily with changes in temperature and precipitation regime. 
The İstanbul CCAP Climate Hazard Risk 
Assessment and the accompanying academic papers and Climate Scenarios report have comprehensively evaluated the different 
Representative 
Concentration 
Pathways (RCPs), namely the impact of radiative forcing in 2100 on future climate changes on a 
W/m2 scale. CCAP used downscaled global climate models and national data inputs and outputs from stations operated by the General Directorate of 
Meteorology (MGM) provided thirty years of city-specific climate data. This modeling approach considered possible future climate hazards according to four climate scenarios, namely RCP 2,6, RCP 
4,5, RCP 6,0 and RCP 8,5, as defined in the IPCC's 5th Assessment Report published in 2013.
In 2017, the MGM compared the results of three different global climate models (HadGEM2-ES, 
MPIESMMR and 
GFDL-ESM2M) in order to obtain forecasts for regional climate changes in Türkiye according to RCP scenario 
4,5-8,5 from 2016 to 2099. In all scenarios, İstanbul is expected to istik.aspx?m=İSTANBUL istik.aspx?m=İSTANBUL

1.2.3. Ecosystems and Biodiversity İstanbul has an extraordinary plant richness due to its soil diversity, geographical location between two seas and two continents (under the influence of the humid and cold climate from the 
Black Sea coast and the warmer 
Mediterranean climate of the Marmara 
Sea), topography and traditional land use dating back centuries. 270 plant species growing naturally within the provincial border of İstanbul are included in the "Türkiye's List of Rare and Endemic Plants in Danger. Among them, the richest populations of 40 species in the world are found in İstanbul. Based on the floristic studies conducted by the Wildlife Conservation 
Society and the İstanbul University 
Faculty of Pharmacy, Department of 
Pharmaceutical Botany, 7 important plant areas (IPAs) have been determined within the province of İstanbul.14

1.2.5. Industry and

According to the values announced for 
2019, the sectoral structure of the İstanbul’s employment is approximately 
32% industry, 67% services and 1% agriculture19.
60% of the existing industry in Türkiye is located in the Marmara Region and İstanbul is a city where almost all kinds of industries are located. The fact that İstanbul received a significant share from the infrastructure investments, loans and incentives and public investments required for the industry, especially in transportation and energy, led to a very rapid development in the manufacturing industry. There are 8 organized industrial zones in İstanbul. There are 113 small industrial estates in İstanbul and 
150.000 people are employed in 35.000 businesses located in these estates.
Apart from industrial sites, there are three free zones in İstanbul: Bakırköy, Çatalca and Tuzla. According to TurkStat data, while Türkiye exported 171,46 billion USD in 2019, a portion of 85,63 billion USD, which corresponds to 49,94% of this export, was provided by İstanbul. 
On the import side, while Türkiye realized 
202,70 billion USD of imports in 2019, 
105,26 billion USD of which corresponds to 52% of it was realized by İstanbul20.

National Energy Efficiency Action Plan
Within the scope of the National Energy 
Efficiency Action Plan to be implemented between 2017-2023, it is aimed to reduce 
Türkiye's primary energy consumption by 
14% in 2023 with 55 actions defined in 6 categories, including buildings and services, energy, transportation, industry and technology, agriculture and horizontal issues. It is envisaged to save 
23,9 MTEP cumulatively by 2023 and to invest USD 10,9 billion for this saving.

USD, and the effect of some actions will continue until 204022.

1.3.1. National and International Framework 
Among the many institutions and organizations in Türkiye, two stand out the most in energy efficiency and climate change actions, namely, Ministry of 
Energy and Natural Resources (MENR) and Climate Change Presidency, an affiliated institution of the Ministry of 
Environment, Urbanization and Climate 
Change (MoEUCC).
MENR is incharge of preparing the drafts of legislation, strategies and action plans related to energy efficiency together with regulatory impact analyses, monitoring and evaluating their implementation and planning remedial measures. It is also responsible for determining the energy saving potentials throughout the country and on the basis of sectors, carrying out the necessary studies, including the creation of alternative fuels, district heating and heat market, to make or have energy studies done.
Climate 
Change 
Presidency is responsible for determining policies, strategies, and actions at the national and international level, conducting negotiation processes, and ensuring https://iklim.gov.tr/en/about-us-i-75 https://enerji.gov.tr/bilgi-merkezi-enerji-verimliligi-ulusal-enerji-verimliligi-eylem-plani

National Energy Plan
The Turkish 
National 
Energy 
Plan includes the steps to be taken until 2035, based on Türkiye's target of achieving net zero emissions by 2053. According to the plan, Türkiye's energy consumption, which was 147,2 million tons of oil equivalent in 2020, is projected to reach 
205,3 million tons of oil equivalent in 
2035, meaning a 39,5% increase, in line with 
Türkiye's growth targets. 
Renewable energy sources, which had a 
16.7% share in primary energy consumption in 2020, will increase to 
23,7% in 2035. The country's installed power in electricity will reach 189.700 megawatts (MW), up from 95.900 MW in 2020. 74,3% of this capacity increase is expected to come from renewable energy sources, primarily solar and wind. 
In 2035, the solar installed power will reach 52.900 MW, while this number will be 29.600 MW for wind energy, 35.100 
MW for hydroelectricity, and 5.100 MW for geothermal and biomass23 energy.

A market infrastructure will be established for demand-side participation in electricity and natural gas.

Target (2.3)
Studies will be carried out to increase public awareness of energy efficiency.
•
Target (2.4)
Energy system planning for electric vehicles will be made.

1.3.2. İstanbul Metropolitan 
Municipality
In Türkiye, metropolitan municipalities are upper-tier municipalities that were introduced to the 
Turkish local administration system in 1984. Metropolitan municipalities were established based on the provision "Special forms of administration can be established in large settlements" in article 127 of the 1982 constitution. With the Decree Law No. 195 on the 
Management of 
Metropolitan 
Municipalities, a two-stage metropolitan municipality system was introduced in three provincial centers in March 1984. With the 
Metropolitan Municipality Law No. 3030 and its implementing regulation, which was enacted in the same year, the institutional and legal foundations of the system were strengthened. With the law numbered 6360 enacted in 2012, the areas of responsibility of the metropolitan municipalities were extended to the provincial administrative borders, and all of the villages and districts were included in the metropolitan responsibility. Therefore, municipal service is carried out by both the metropolitan municipality and the district municipality at the same time.
İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality (IMM) is the state institution responsible for municipal affairs of the province of İstanbul. 
35% (1869 km²) of the total land area of İstanbul, which is within the jurisdiction of 
Sustainable Energy and Climate Action Plan of İstanbul 46 https://iklim.gov.tr/en/action-plans-i-121 https://iklim.gov.tr/turkiye-ulusal-katki-beyani-ni-cop27-de-acikladi-haber-84#:~:text=Paris%20Anla%C5%9Fmas%C4%B1 %20%C3%A7er%C3%A7evesinde%202030%20y%C4%B1l%C4%B1,%C3%A7er %C3%A7evesinde%20y%C3%BCzde%2041'e%20y%C3%BCkseltilmi%C5%9Ftir.

[Page 49]
the İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality, is located on the Anatolian Side and 65% (3474 km²) is on the European side28.
İstanbul 
Metropolitan 
Municipality is divided into three main groups: İstanbul 
Metropolitan Municipality Mayor, İstanbul 
Metropolitan Municipality Council and İstanbul 
Metropolitan 
Municipality 
Committee.
According to the 26th article of the 
Metropolitan Municipality Law No. 5216 published in the Official Gazette dated 
23.07.2004 and numbered 24431, the 
Metropolitan Municipality can establish capital companies in the fields of duty and service assigned to it, in accordance with the procedures specified in the relevant legislation. IMM carries many services through these special-purpose organizations, which have a separate legal personality and budget, are semi-commercial, but are subordinate to the local government and operate under the general control of the local government29. The İstanbul Metropolitan 
Municipality governs İstanbul with 30 affiliated companies and 2 subsidiaries.
Again, within the scope of Law No. 
521630 Metropolitan Municipalities have been given the following duties, powers and responsibilities, which are related to sustainable energy and climate change actions:
To prepare, have it made and to approve and implement a master development plan in every scale between 1/5,000 and 1/25,000 within the boundaries of the metropolitan municipality, provided that it is in accordance with the environmental plan; to approve the implementation development plans to be prepared by the municipalities in the metropolitan area in accordance with the master plan, the changes to be made in these plans, the parceling plans and the development improvement plans as they are or by changing them, and to supervise their implementation;
To make or have and implement the metropolitan transportation master plan; planning and coordinating transportation and public transportation services;
To ensure the protection of the environment, agricultural areas and water basins in accordance with the principle of sustainable development; afforestation; to gather unsanitary workplaces, entertainment venues, and other workplaces that have an impact on public health and the environment in certain parts of the city; determining construction İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality

[Page 50]
materials, scrap storage areas and sales places, excavation soil, rubble, sand and gravel storage areas, wood and coal sales and storage areas, and taking measures that will not cause environmental pollution in their transportation; to make and have the metropolitan solid waste management plan made; to perform services related to the reuse, storage and disposal of solid wastes and excavations, except for the collection of solid wastes at the source and transportation to the transfer station, to establish facilities for this purpose, to have them installed, to operate or to have them operated; to carry out services related to industrial and medical wastes, to establish the necessary facilities for this, to have them installed, to operate or to have them operated; collecting, collecting, treating the wastes of marine vehicles and making the necessary arrangements.
To carry out public transportation services within the metropolitan area and to establish, have them set up, operate or have them operated for this purpose, to give licenses to public transportation vehicles, including taxis and service vehicles, in the land and sea within the metropolitan borders.
Regarding the public transportation lines within the metropolitan area; 
To decide on the operation of public transportation services related to the lines to be determined based on the criteria of distance to the city center, population and number of people using the line.
To carry out water and sewerage services, to establish dams and other facilities necessary, to have them installed and operated; reclamation of streams; marketing spring water or water produced at the end of treatment.
To make plans and other preparations for natural disasters at the metropolitan scale in accordance with the plans made at the provincial level; providing tools, equipment and material support to other disaster areas when necessary; conducting fire and emergency services; to detect explosive and flammable material production and storage areas, to inspect residences, workplaces, entertainment venues, factories and industrial establishments and public institutions in terms of precautions to be taken against fire and other disasters, to issue permits and licenses required by the legislation in this regard.

Local governments are subject to the country's policies when it comes to energy resources such as electricity and natural gas consumed in the city. This is due to 
Law No 5216, which does not assign any duties to metropolitan municipalities in this regard.
 
1.3.3. IMM’s Climate Resilience 
Activities İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality is a member and/or active participant in some regional/inter-regional local governments climate networks related to climate change. Most notable of them are the 
Global Covenant of Mayors (GCoM), 
Large Cities Climate Leadership Group (C40) and UCLG-MEWA Environmental 
Committee. İstanbul 
Metropolitan 
Municipality is the first and only municipality from Türkiye to be a member of the C40 Cities Climate Leadership 
Group.
Since İstanbul is a city that is acutely aware of the vulnerability created by climate change, IMM can be considered one of the first local administrations in the world to start research on climate change adaptation. The strategy and action plan of the İstanbul International 
Financial Center, prepared in 2009, includes the goal of establishing a carbon exchange program for İstanbul. 
To achieve this goal, the plan includes actions by the İstanbul Metropolitan 
Municipality to implement low-emission projects and promote the creation of a local carbon market. Within that context, 
IMM prepared a greenhouse gas emission inventory in 2010.
Later, the Metropolitan Administration announced the amount of carbon dioxide emissions in accordance with international standards in April 2013. In 
2015, the İstanbul 
Metropolitan 
Municipality revised its GHG inventory of electricity, transport, natural gas, waste and other fuels and took steps for the preparation of İstanbul Climate Change 
Action Plan (2015-2018). During this process, important studies were carried out, which are the basis and source of the İstanbul Climate Change Action Plan, the main ones being the Climate Scenarios and the 
Climate 
Change 
Risks, 
Opportunities and Vulnerabilities Analysis 
Report. 
As of 
2016, İstanbul 
Metropolitan 
Municipality is a member of the "Global 
Covenant of Mayors for Climate and 
Energy-GCoM", an international climate network committed to voluntarily reducing greenhouse gas emissions and becoming a resilient city. At the C40 
Mayors 
Summit in 
Copenhagen in 
October 2019, IMM Mayor Mr. Ekrem İmamoğlu signed the "Deadline 2020" 
Commitment and the goal of making İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality

[Page 52]
İstanbul a "carbon neutral" and "resilient city" by 2050 has been accepted. With technical support from C40, İstanbul's 
Climate Change Action Plan was revised and shared with the public in 2021.
The highest share in the sectoral distribution of the greenhouse gas emission inventory prepared by IMM every year belongs to stationary energy. For this reason, IMM has started the SECAP preparation process, being aware of the need for İstanbul to focus on the stationary energy sector in order to reduce its greenhouse gas emissions.

1.3.4. Methodological Approach for SECAP Preparation
During the preparation of the SECAP, a process integration approach was employed to ensure that the project processes were considered holistically. Plan preparation and approval were synchronized and connected to capacity building activities, which were in turn synchronized and connected to coordination and communication activities. This approach allowed for the integration of individual tasks to improve the overall efficiency of the project. Figure 3 illustrates the key processes in the project design.
İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality

[Page 54]
transport. 
SECAP should concentrate on measures aimed to increase share of renewables reducing 
GHG emissions such as carbon dioxide (CO2), and the final energy consumption by end users, as well as include adaptation actions in response to the impacts of 
Climate 
Change. 
The 
Covenant of Mayors for Climate and 
Energy (CoM) requires local authorities to take action to reduce their greenhouse gas emissions and to increase their energy efficiency. 
This commitment applies to the entire geographical area of the local authority, including both public and private sectors. As such, the 
Sustainable Energy and Climate Action 
Plan (SECAP) should include actions for both sectors. The local authority is expected to lead by example and take ambitious measures to reduce emissions from its own buildings, facilities, and vehicle fleet etc32.
IMM already prepared and disclosed İstanbul Climate Change Action Plan (CCAP) in 2021 in line with C40 Cities33. 
This CCAP outlines the necessary steps for the entire city to take in order to combat climate change.
SECAPs have 3 fundamental targets (Chatelet, 201831):
1. Mitigation (accelerating the transition to low carbon in the region), mitigating 
CO2 emissions by minimum 40% by 
2030 with the renewable energy and energy efficiency measures.
2. Adaptation (increasing the capacity of adaptation towards inevitable impacts of climate change), being prepared against the change that will be brought by global warming and creating a resilient structure.
3. Sustainable Energy (energy efficiency and renewable energy), developing cooperation with other local administrations in the region for access to reliable and suitably priced energy.
SECAP defines the activities and measures set up to achieve the targets, together with time frames and assigned responsibilities. Covenant Signatories are free to choose the format of their SECAP, as long as it is in line with the general principles set out in the Covenant of 
Mayors Office guidelines. 
For Climate Change mitigation, the main target sectors are buildings, equipment/facilities and urban

The procedure of preparation, development, implementation and monitoring of the 
SECAP was divided into the following main steps: 
The SECAP document comprises of six sub-reports which were developed as per sub-working group outputs and combined to form the full SECAP report. These sub-reports are namely BEI, RVA, buildings, transport, and waste/waste water sectors, and scenarios.
Sub-working groups were formed with appropriate and relevant units and institutions to work under these headings. The sub-working group, under the direction of the head, began the process of preparing the sub-reports by collecting data and information. 
During the workshops held, technical information and guidelines for drafting the sub-report were shared, in accordance with the SECAP format. The sub-reports included literature, current and calculation methods, and modelling (if applicable), as well as information and material that would be used in the mitigation, monitoring, and adaptation sections.
The sub-reports were distributed to the relevant stakeholders for their review and feedback. Upon completion of the review process, the sub-reports were consolidated to create a comprehensive SECAP report. The SECAP preparation spanned a period of 24 months, beginning in July 2021 and concluding in June 2023.

Covenant’s transport, waste and water – waste water sectors were presented to the participants with a focus on Climate Change mitigation and adaptation actions. 
Risks and vulnerabilities to climate change, identifying and discussing adaptation options were also assessed.
In the discussion part, the following topics were discussed:
1. What are the current climate risks and environmental problems in İstanbul due to global warming and what are the possible climate risks and environmental problems that may arise in the future, especially in the light of climate change? What can be done about these identified challenges?
2. If you examine a map showing the river drainage systems of İstanbul, where could be the most vulnerable areas and districts in terms of flooding and overland flows. 
What could be the possible reasons for this? What can be done to solve these challenges?
3. If you were given a mandate to solve environmental problems in İstanbul, which problem or problems would you like to start with first? And why? What can be done to solve these environmental challenges?

Consultation With Key Stakeholders and 
Meeting For Determination Of Climate 
Change Adaptation Options
Consultation with key stakeholders and meeting for Determination of Climate 
Change Adaptation Options for İstanbul was held on 19-20-21.09.2022. An average of 40 participants attended every day. The consultations aimed at supporting İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality, with its efforts to determine priority actions for adaptation to climate change that can address biodiversity loss, heat islands, city and coastal floods in an efficient and cost-effective way. The meeting was organized according to the 
SECAP methodology to define climate change adaptation actions and will discuss on the best possible adaptation actions together with the relevant stakeholders and decision-maker institutions. The target participants of the meetings were the key stakeholders from IMM's relevant units, related 
IMM-affiliated/ subsidiary companies, relevant district municipalities in İstanbul, and representatives of relevant central government institutions. 
The consultations were completed in a total of six sessions, one in the morning and one in the afternoon on each day. The "Multiple 
Criteria Analysis" method was used to determine the importance of the five adaptation measures determined as a result of the survey application. In order to perform multiple criteria analysis, a previously prepared "Multiple Criteria 
Determination Table" was distributed to the participants. In determining the order of importance, the contribution of the relevant adaptation methods to prevent life and economic losses, cost and applicability criteria were taken into consideration. 
In the morning session of the first day (19.9.2022), various maps showing the vulnerability of İstanbul in terms of habitat and floods were introduced to the participants. 
Discussions with participants about the maps led to an attempt to recognize İstanbul's vulnerabilities. 
Following the map presentation, a presentation on urban flooding and the typical strategies adopted to prevent or lessen its effects was given during the second half of the morning session. A further presentation was also given on the possible adaptation methods to adapt to floods in coastal areas of İstanbul. In the afternoon session of the first day and based on the presentation, a group work was carried out with the same participants. This work involved participants to identify the five districts of İstanbul that are most susceptible to flooding as well as the names of the five best methods for these districts to adapt to flooding.
On the second day (20.9.2022), all of the activities focused on the quality and İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality

[Page 60]
degradation of biodiversity in İstanbul and its districts. A presentation on "Climate 
Change Adaptation Action Planning" was presented for this purpose. Following that, another presentation was given about the adaptation measures for biodiversity loss in İstanbul, as well as the advantages and disadvantages of each measure. After the presentations, a group work was carried out in order to identify the priority areas of İstanbul in terms of biodiversity loss. 
Within the scope of this group work, a survey was conducted to identify the five districts in İstanbul with the greatest loss of biodiversity (Habitat) and the five appropriate adaptation methods to minimize habitat destruction in these districts. 
On the third day (21.9.2022), "Reducing 
Losses and Damages Caused by Heat 
Islands," on adaptation planning for the impacts of climate change and İstanbul's adaptation options to the heat island impact were presented. Then, heat islands and adaptation options and their advantages, disadvantages and suitability for İstanbul were discussed with participants. After the discussion session, a group work was conducted with the participants. A survey was carried out as part of this group study to identify the names of the five districts that experience the greatest heat island effect and to identify the names of the five most effective adaptation strategies from the adaptation table presented to them in order to mitigate the heat island effects in these districts.
Sustainable Urban Mobility Plan (SUMP)

One of the main policies to achieve the goal of the İstanbul Climate Change

SUMP.

2. BASELINE EMISSION INVENTORY of CO2 emissions in the territory and indicate priorities to reduce them overall. 
If actions to reduce CO2- equivalent greenhouse gases (GHGs) are covered by the SECAP, the inventory should also include methane (CH4) and nitrous oxide (N2O) emissions. These inventories are not only essential to develop SECAP targets; they are powerful motivators to all parties involved as evidence of the impacts of their efforts. The three main scopes of 
GHG emissions to be potentially included in the emission inventory are provided in Figure 5.
2.1. 
INTRODUCTION
The Baseline Emissions Inventory (BEI) quantifies the amount of CO2 emitted due to energy production and consumption, fuel/electricity consumption in transportation, waste and waste water management in the territory under the responsibility of the local authority in the baseline year selected. It is required for 
Global Covenant of Mayors (GCoM) and 
EU Covenant of Mayors (CoM) signatories to determine the relative emissions reduction target relative to baseline year. 
Elaborating a BEI is of critical importance as the inventory will be the instrument allowing the local authority to measure the impact of its actions related to climate change. The BEI will show where the local authority was at the beginning, and the successive monitoring of emission inventories will show the progress towards the objective. Emission inventories are very important elements to maintain the motivation of all parties willing to contribute to the local authority's CO2 reduction objective, allowing them to see the results of their efforts. 
The BEI will identify the principal human-caused (anthropogenic) sources 
Direct emissions due to final energy consumption
The quantification of GHG emissions (mainly CO2) due to energy consumption in both stationary and mobile sources is mandatory in the reporting.  İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality

[Page 68]
SELECTION 
OF 
SECTORS 
INCLUDED 
Local authorities may include all sectors relevant to their circumstances. 
However, local authorities shall report 
GHG emissions at least from three main sectors, namely buildings/stationary energy, transportation, and waste/other non-energy related. Table 2. SECAP 
Sectors Considered in the BEI Table 2 shows the sectors included in the BEI studies for IMM.
Indirect emissions related to consumption of grid-supplied energy (electricity and heating/cooling) within the local territory boundary
The emissions from energy generation activities within the local territory boundaries are accounted for through the local emission factor for electricity and for heating/cooling. 
Non-energy related emissions that occur in the local territory
It is mandatory to include in the emission inventory the waste and other non-energy related greenhouse gas emissions.

Energy Supply İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality

[Page 72]
COLLECTION 
AND 
EMISSION INVENTORY 
In the compilation of BEI, the following concepts are of utmost importance: 
1. 
Baseline year, is the year against which the achievements of the emission reductions in 2030 shall be compared. 
The EU has committed to reduce the emissions by 40% by 2030 compared to 
1990, and 1990 is also the base year of the Kyoto Protocol. To be able to compare the emission reduction of the 
EU and the Covenant signatories, a common base year is needed, and therefore 1990 is the recommended baseline year of the BEI. However, if the local authority does not have data to compile an inventory for 1990, then it should choose the closest subsequent year for which the most comprehensive and reliable data can be collected.
İstanbul 
Metropolitan 
Municipality signed the Deadline 2020 commitment to be a Carbon Neutral city by 2050 in 
2019 and revised the İstanbul Climate 
Change Action Plan according to the new targets of the Commitment. In the report shared with the public in 2021. 
2019 was taken as the reference/base year and the Greenhouse Gas Inventory of İstanbul was prepared. Therefore, 
2019 was taken as the reference year for the "Sustainable Energy and Climate 
Action Plan (SECAP) study." 
2. 
Activity data, was used both in the province and in institutional inventory calculations. The amount of energy means the amount of fuel converted to 
MWh. Examples of activity data used are: 
•
Natural Gas used in residential buildings 
•
Electricity consumed in municipal buildings and in the province
•
Fuel consumed by vehicles 
•
The amount of waste water treated by biological and physical means
•
Amount of waste disposed of and/or recycled
3. 
Emission factors, are coefficients which quantify the emission per unit of activity. The emissions are estimated by multiplying the emission factor with corresponding activity data. Examples of emission factors are: 
•
Amount of CO2 emitted per MWh of natural gas consumed 
•
Amount of CO2 emitted per MWh electricity consumed 
•
Amount of CO2 emitted per MWh fuel consumed

2.4. 
EMISSION FACTORS
The GHG emission calculations within the determined responsibliities of IMM were performed using a formula shown below, adapted from the Intergovernmental Panel on 
Climate Change (IPCC) “Tier-1” and “Tier-2” methodology. It is consistent with the formula provided in the Guidebook “How to develop a Sustainable Energy and Climate 
Action Plan (SECAP)”37, which is based on activity data on fuel consumption and emission factors: s = sector f = energy carrier
Fuel consumption data given as unit mass or volume are multiplied by net calorific values and converted to MWh. 
Three greenhouse gases were included in the inventory: CO2, NH4 and N2O. NH4 and N2O emissions sourcing from solid waste disposal activities were calculated separately. 
Global warming potentials of GHGs included in the inventory were taken from IPCC 5th 
Assessment Report (AR5)38 
The emission factors which are used in this SECAP were applied from IPCC Emission 
Factors except the national electricity grid emission factor calculated for Türkiye39. 
The average emission factor from electricity is taken from the Primary Energy and 
Greenhouse Gas Emission Coefficients of Electricity published by the Ministry of Energy and Natural Resources.

2.5. 
CALCULATION APPROACH
GHG emission calculation approach for the baseline emissions inventory is performed according to Greenhouse Gas Protocol (GHG Protocol)40 developed by the World 
Resources Institute (WRI) and the World Business Council for Sustainable Development (WBCSD). In the GHG Protocol, release categories are classified as follows: 
•
Scope 1 – direct greenhouse gas emissions: Emissions from all fixed and mobile emission sources (Table 1.4) owned or directly controlled by the organization. These resources include assets owned, leased or financed. The scope limit is all controllable emission sources. 
•
Scope 2 – indirect energy greenhouse gas emissions: Emissions from energy purchased for the organization's activities, mainly electricity from the grid including electricity or other types of energy used for heating/cooling purposes. 
•
Scope 3 – other indirect greenhouse gas emissions: Indirect greenhouse gas emissions resulting from the activities of the organization but not directly under the control of the organization. 
Like in the city inventory, as per GPC basic standards in IMM GHG inventory Scope 3 was also excluded and not calculated. Table 4 shows the difference between scopes at municipal and urban scale.

Four categories have been identified as the city-wide sources of GHG emissions related to waste and waste water: 
•
Solid waste disposal: treatment and disposal of solid waste in landfills generates significant amount of methane through anaerobic decomposition
•
Biological treatment: biological treatment of organic waste, such as food waste, garden waste and sludge emit both nitrous oxide and methane. 
Composting and anaerobic digestion are included in this category
•
Waste incineration: carbon in solid waste that is of non-biogenic origin generates carbon dioxide while burning
•
Waste water treatment and discharge: organic material in domestic waste water generates methane due to anaerobic decomposition. Nitrous oxide 
• emissions from waste water treatment can occur as direct emissions from treatment plants.
Stationary energy sector emissions comprise 62,9% of all GHG emissions in İstanbul city. The majority of stationary energy sector emissions come from the residential buildings (14,1 
MtCO2e), predominantly due to natural gas use in homes for cooking, hot water and heating.

GHG emissions of this sector respectively.
The transportation sector is responsible for 
28,3% of total emissions and the single most significant source is on-road private and commercial transportation. 
This activity comes mostly from private cars and a significant number of buses. The 2019 inventory includes emissions from rail and water-borne navigation, but these represent less than 
1% of total transportation emissions.
Waste sector, which is controlled and managed by IMM through company İSTAÇ, represents the remaining 8,8% of emissions in İstanbul, with the vast majority (89,4%) of these coming from solid waste disposal. The remaining 10,6% of waste emissions are allocated across waste water treatment (5,4%), industrial incineration (2,7%) and biological treatment (composting) (2,5%).

INTRODUCTION
Controlling climate change effects also requires an in-depth assessment of impacts, vulnerabilities, and solutions for disaster risk reduction, enhancing the resilience of human systems and ecosystems and developing nature-based solutions. 
From this perspective, two key concepts emerge the implementation of a circular economy and the increase of the resilience of cities and communities. 
The greater a system’s ability to conserve its energy supply, the more adaptable it will be to internal and external events (such as climate change or other threats). a resilient approach considers Vulnerability and multiple-risk assessment in cities to define spatial policies. Resilient solutions are designed to address Adaptation to climate change by using nature-based solutions to achieve a measurable benefit of socio-economic health. Adaptation and risk management policies and practices will be more successful if they consider the dynamic nature of vulnerability and exposure. 
According to a 2016 study conducted by 
Basque Center for Climate Change in 
Spain44 , which ranks coastal cities in 
Europe based on their risks, İstanbul ranks first and İzmir third.
As claimed in the İstanbul Climate 
Change Action Plan Summary Report (2018) İstanbul is one of the European cities most vulnerable to climate change, thus adaptation activities have priority. In 2017, Türkiye experienced one of its driest years. In the summer of the same year, heavy rainfall and severe hail events have affected the transportation infrastructure and caused significant damage to homes and vehicles. According to a study that assesses the potential economic damages of extreme weather events in 
15 European coastal cities, İstanbul ranks first45 . 
In the report, it is explicitly written that, despite minor uncertainties which depend on modeling gaps, İstanbul’s climate will converge to a typical 
Mediterranean climate which means that it will become much warmer and drier. Moreover, the Mediterranean basin will become one of the most vulnerable to climate change due to the position, the number of coasts, and the exposure of people.
Particularly, the emerging issues for İstanbul are found to be the change in temperature and precipitation 
L. M. Abadie, E. Sainz de Murieta and I. Galarraga, “Climate Risk Assessment under Uncertainty: An Application to Main European Coastal Cities,” Frontiers in Marine 
Science, Volume 3, p. 265, 2016.
İstanbul Climate Change Action Plan Summary Report, Department of 
Environmental Protection and Development Directorate of Environmental Protection İBB 
Beyoğlu Ek Hizmet Binası Hacı Ahmet Mah. Kurtuluş Deresi Cad. Yeni Yol Zarif Sok. No: 22,

Mah. Piyalepaşa Blv. No: 74 34379 Şişli / İSTANBUL
Sustainable Energy and Climate Action Plan of İstanbul 86

[Page 89]
patterns, drought, increased sea levels, and cross-cutting themes related to urban heat islands and air quality. 
The average temperatures in İstanbul are expected to rise until the end of the century. This increase will be higher during summer months, which will be amplified significantly by the urban heat island effect and potential heat waves. While the total precipitation is expected to decrease slightly, heavy rainfall is expected in shorter periods of time, leading to potential flash floods. 
The severity and frequency of extreme climatic events are expected to increase. Some examples are already experienced in recent years in İstanbul in the form of floods, droughts, and hailstorms. 
Four main changes in the normal pattern are identified: higher average temperatures, shift in wet and dry seasons, higher tides and erosion rates, variations in annual precipitation, excessive evaporation, and mean sea level rise (Figure 9). Therefore, the change in extreme patterns will include heat waves, excessive evapotranspiration, epidemics, droughts, floods and landslides, and storm surges.
According to these statements, the four ecosystem vulnerability maps should be interpreted as a baseline guide for the future Adaptation of the city. Changes in precipitation, temperature and pollutants will obviously affect the biodiversity, the erosion/inundation of the coastline, the diffusion of urban heat islands, and the possibility of being affected by pluvial floodings.

METHODOLOGICAL NOTES
In order to model the ecosystem vulnerabilities of İstanbul city, InVEST model46, which is a suite of free, open-source software models used to map and value the goods and services from nature that sustain and fulfill human life, was used. 
InVEST's versatile modular design feature has made it an important modular program used in different fields. lnVEST assists decision makers in the quantitative evaluation of the areas where it is applied and provides advantages in establishing the planned regulations on more concrete foundations. The toolset includes distinct ecosystem service models designed for terrestrial, freshwater, marine, and coastal ecosystems, as well as several “helper tools” to assist with locating and processing input data and with understanding and visualizing outputs.
InVEST models are spatially-explicit, using maps as information sources and producing maps as outputs. InVEST models are distributed as a standalone application independent of a GIS software. You need mapping software such as QGIS or ArcGIS to view results. 
As an outcome of the İstanbul Climate 
Change Action Plan47, four climate change risks were selected to be throughly studied for adaptation actions. These are: 
1. 
Urban Floods
2. 
Coastal Floods
3. 
Habitat Degradation
4. 
Heat Islands
The 
InVEST model was used to determine and evaluate in which districts or regions of İstanbul the above-mentioned risk factors are concentrated, or rather their spatial distribution.
Habitat Quality
The InVEST Habitat Quality model uses habitat quality and rarity as proxies to represent the biodiversity of a landscape, estimating the extent of habitat and vegetation types across a landscape and their state of degradation. The model combines maps of land use land cover (LULC) with data on threats to habitats and habitat response. Modeling habitat quality alongside ecosystem services enables users to compare spatial patterns and identify areas where conservation will most benefit natural systems and protect threatened species. This model does not attempt to place a monetary value on biodiversity.
İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality https://invest-userguide.readthedocs.io/en/latest/index.html https://cevre.ibb.istanbul/wp-content/uploads/2022/01/ist_iklim_degisikligi_eylem_plani.pdf

[Page 92]
Sustainable Energy and Climate Action Plan of İstanbul 
Urban Flood Risk Mitigation
Flood hazard comes from different sources, including riverine (or fluvial) flooding, stormwater (or urban) flooding, and coastal flooding. Natural infrastructure can play a role in each of these. Related to stormwater flooding - the focus of the InVEST flood risk mitigation model, natural infrastructure operates mainly by: reducing runoff production, slowing surface flows, and creating space for water (in floodplains or basins). The 
InVEST model calculates the runoff reduction, i.e. the amount of runoff retained per pixel compared to the storm volume. It also calculates, for each watershed, the potential economic damage by overlaying information on flood extent potential and built infrastructure.
Urban Cooling (Heat Islands)
Urban heat mitigation is a priority for many cities that have undergone heat waves in recent years. Vegetation can help reduce the urban heat island by providing shade, modifying the thermal properties of the urban fabric, and increasing cooling through evapotranspiration. 
This has consequences for the health and wellbeing of citizens through reduced mortality and morbidity, increased comfort and productivity, and reduced need for air conditioning. The InVEST urban cooling model calculates an index of heat mitigation based on shade, evapotranspiration, and albedo, as well as distance from cooling islands (e.g. parks).
Coastal Vulnerability
The InVEST Coastal Vulnerability model uses geophysical and natural habitat characteristics of coastal landscapes to compare their exposure to erosion and flooding in severe weather. When overlaid with data on coastal population density, the model’s outputs can be used to identify where humans face higher risks of damage from storm waves and surge.

[Page 93]
VULNERABILITIES OF İSTANBUL CITY 
3.3.1. Urban Flood Risk
Cloudburst events represent one of the most potentially dangerous conditions for coastal cities worldwide. In fact, climate change is rapidly augmenting the number of extreme events while exposing the densely inhabited coastal areas to potential damage and dangerous situations. 
Besides, mountainous coastal cities have an idiosyncratic vulnerability to be flooded, which is intended as an urban system's propensity to be inundated during a cloudburst event. 
In particular, numerous high-density Mediterranean urban areas are developed on hillside terrains along the coastline, on poorly hydraulically conductible soils, with a limited capacity to drain the rainwater and little favorable terrain to create run-off streams.
The urban flood mitigation model represents one of the first explicitly designed tools for urban mapping vulnerabilities. The model assumes that flood-prone areas are impermeable material of artificial surfaces built upon low drainage soils. The model considers that the water on the impervious surface moves directly to the area next to it, contributing directly to surface flow accumulation. The exact biophysical estimation of the run-off in urban land can be significantly difficult to compute since the discharge volume during a cloudburst can be affected by many factors (quantity, quality, and surface of buildings, sewer systems capacity, the presence of dust/leaves in the ground).
The output calculates the run-off retention index (i.e., the percentage of run-off retained per pixel compared to the storm volume) and, on the contrary, the millimeters of water that undergoes the run-off process. The land use classification was entirely built around the United States Department of 
Agriculture (USDA) classes, employing the 
Imperviousness 
High-Resolution 
Layer (HRL) database48.
The model produced the following results in Table 7 and in Figure 10.

The districts in İstanbul are listed according to their vulnerability level in 
Table 7, the most vulnerable districts being Güngören and Gaziosmanpaşa. 
When the flow retention index is high, the ability to contain and direct water increases, which can reduce the risk of flooding. A low flow detention index can cause water to accumulate rapidly and increase the risk of flooding. 
Accordingly, districts with low flow retention index in the table above can be described as vulnerable to flood risk.
In order to explain how the data Table 7 should be read, Bağcılar district is taken as an example. According to this data, 
Bağcılar district is the fourth most critical district in terms of run-off retention. According to the modelling output, only 48% of the rain volume (of a simulated single rain event of 50mm), can be retained by the soil. It means that most of the rain volume (52%) will undergo a run-off process. 
More specifically, the model calculated that in that specific flash flood event (50mm) the potential flood volume is 580.393,29 m3. 
Then, to empirically measure if these results can be considered sustainable for the district, the discharge rainwater capacity of the channel in the district should be evaluated against the results to see if potential stormwater floods can occur during huge rain events in the city.
Figure 10 shows the pluvial flooding vulnerability levels in İstanbul. A low score in a grid cell (darker shade on the map) means urban flooding risk in the cell is high relative to other cells. If the southern parts of the basin sections where the population and settlement are intense are excluded, the rivers partially preserve their semi-natural characteristics in the northern regions that form the upper parts of the river basins. Stream bed patterns also show significant changes in the southern parts where urbanization and population are dense. In these areas, the riverbeds partially preserve their natural characteristics because they are semi-canalized by human intervention. 
The more the riverbeds are changed by unconscious anthropogenic interventions, the more fragility of those rivers in terms of floods increases. In the case of flash floods, if the anthropic system has a failure, the probability of huge inundations is high. 
While considering how to adapt the system to this specific problem (pluvial flooding), it is important to give priority to all those areas that present a huge İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality

[Page 96]
vulnerability and then, once the critical areas for intervention are defined, check whether streams or upstreams cross these areas according to the flow accumulation analysis.

3.3.2. Coastal Vulnerability Model
Faced with an intensification of human activities and a changing climate, coastal communities need to better understand how modifications of the biological and physical environment (i.e. direct and indirect removal of natural habitats for coastal development) can affect their exposure to storm-induced erosion and flooding (inundation). The 
InVEST Coastal Vulnerability model produces a qualitative estimate of such exposure in terms of a vulnerability index, differentiating areas with relatively high or low exposure to erosion and inundation during storms. 
By coupling these results with global population information, the model can show areas along a given coastline where humans are most vulnerable to storm waves and storm surge. The model does not consider coastal processes that are unique to a region, nor does it predict long- or short-term changes in shoreline position or configuration. The results provide a qualitative representation of coastal hazard risks, rather than quantifying shoreline retreat or inundation limits.
The model calculates the index of coastal exposure to storm-induced erosion and flooding. Figure 11 shows the final outputs of the model. İstanbul has more than one type of coast. Two types of coasts can be seen, especially in İstanbul, divided into Ria and Lagoon-Port Coasts. The coastal type formed by the inundation of river valleys under sea water is called the Ria coastal type. It has the coast type of İstanbul-Çanakkale Strait and Golden 
Horn Ria. On the map, the coasts in 
Sarıyer, Beykoz, and Beyoğlu, Fatih, 
Zeytinburnu, Üsküdar districts, which are places with the Ria coastal type, show high coastal vulnerability distribution with the effect of the Black 
Sea, except 
Beşiktaş, while the south-western part of Beykoz and the northern part of Üsküdar, located in either side of the middle of the 
Bosphorus show low coastal vulnerability. The reason for this is that, since it is between two lands, the salt water inflow is less compared to its surroundings, and the coastal vulnerability such as erosion and storm waves is less than the environment. 
Kadıköy, Maltepe, Kartal, Pendik and 
Tuzla coasts running parallel to 
Heybeliada, 
Adalar, 
Büyükada, 
Kınalıada and 
Golden 
Horn have coastal vulnerability due to factors such as wind, distance, and depth of the 
Marmara Sea.
The other coastal type that is unique to İstanbul is coastal with Lagoon-Harbor. 
As in Büyükçekmece and Küçükçekmece İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality

[Page 98]
examples, when the waves accumulate alluvium in front of a bay, the mouth of the bay is partially or completely closed and lagoon shores are formed. These two coast types have a lower vulnerability index than the other coasts mentioned above. Considering future climate change, districts such as Bakırköy, Küçükçekmece, especially Avcılar and 
Beylikdüzü may be at higher risk of coastal vulnerability.

As for coastal vulnerability, the sea level rise prediction of 75 cm has been used as a new input layer to map this specific vulnerability in the İstanbul metropolitan area. The model has been re-launched using only the scenario evaluation that considers the sea level rise hazard (Figure 12). Results indicate that two coastal hotspots will be threatened with higher intensity: all the Golden Horn coast, including the eastern coast facing the Bosphorus Strait, and the northern coastal areas of the Bosphorus Strait can be impacted by the seal level rise. Since the northern coast is less anthropized, the exposure of people, buildings and other aesthetic and historical values in this part of the metropolitan area is minimized. On the other hand, the extremely vulnerable situation due to sea-level rise in the city's most ancient, historical, dense and touristically attractive area represents a serious problem that should be faced by employing various technique of Adaptation through the re-design and re-develop of the waterfront.

3.3.3. Habitat Quality
InVEST's habitat quality model combines information on land use and land cover (LULC) and biodiversity threats to create habitat quality maps. The habitat degradation section of the model shows the extent and configuration of natural land cover types in the current or potential future landscape relative to the extent of similar natural land cover types at some reference time. 
The first step to modeling consisted in selecting the borders of İstanbul. Then, imperviousness data was selected as a threat to habitat quality and has been clipped according to the study area boundary. The land use data to be used in the sensitivity table was downloaded 
2018. 
Using the zonal statistic as table tool, min, max and mean values of imperviousness were calculated for each land use class, and the database was then post-processed in excel. The relative values of imperviousness were used as a proxy for the evaluation of habitat values (e.g. if dense urban settlements are impervious at 80% it means their degree of naturalness is the opposite – 
20% thus selecting 0.2 as the input value for the Habitat in the sensitivity table of the InVEST model.
The model produced the relative level of habitat quality on the current landscape of İstanbul city as shown in 
Figure 13. Higher numbers (darker shade on the map) indicate better habitat quality vis-a-vis the distribution of habitat quality across the rest of the landscape. Areas on the landscape that are not habitats get a quality score of 
0. This quality score is unitless and does not refer to any particular biodiversity measure.
Likewise, Figure 14 shows the relative level of habitat degradation on the current landscape. A high score in a grid cell (darker shade blue-reddish on the map) means habitat degradation in the cell is high relative to other cells. Grid cells with non-habitat land cover get a degradation score of 0.
Sustainable Energy and Climate Action Plan of İstanbul

Forest, which is surrounded by the residential areas of Kartal, Pendik and 
Sultanbeyli districts, is one of the most sensitive areas in the middle of residential areas, with a value of 0,95.

3.3.4. Urban Heat Island
The 
Urban 
Heat 
Island (UHI) phenomenon is mainly caused by the physical structure of the built environment, which absorbs solar radiation and releases more heat than the natural landscape. The urban microclimate is highly influenced by urban design due to the complex interactions between outdoor conditions (e.g., air temperature, humidity, wind speed, solar radiation) and the morphological parameters shaping cities (e.g., urban density, building form, roads, streets, and canopies geometry, building orientation). The impacts of UHI include the citizens' health (particularly children and the elderly) leading to respiratory and cardiovascular problems, dehydration, heat exhaustion, and potentially life-threatening heatstroke, more than exacerbating existing medical conditions, such as heart and kidney disease.
In the future, the UHI effect will be one of the major challenges to be managed in urban areas; it can be reduced by making planning decisions oriented towards the performance-based design of public and private spaces, for example, ensuring the permeability and the reflectance of paved materials, and introducing densely green planted areas and vegetation which could increase the cooling process due to evapotranspiration and shading surfaces, while absorbing short-wave radiation.
The model produces the following results as shown in Figure 19 and Figure 
20. For the modelling, reference air temperature was inputted as 30°C; the difference between the rural reference temperature and the maximum temperature observed in the city was inputted as 3,5°C. In Figure 19, where the average temperature anomaly is shown, the tangible effect on urban comfort provided by the peri-urban open spaces of the northern seminatural land of the city is visible. 
While obviously, the internal part of the densely inhabited system presents critical values, showing a low cooling capacity. The InVEST urban cooling model calculates an index of heat mitigation (HMI) based on shade, evapotranspiration, and albedo, as well as distance from cooling islands (e.g. parks). The index is used to estimate temperature reduction by vegetation. 
Figure 20 is a map of the modelled HMI for İstanbul city, where the lighter colors show the cooling effect of green areas on temperature. İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality

[Page 110]
Figure 19. Average temperature anomaly of İstanbul City with the InVEST Model
Sustainable Energy and Climate Action Plan of İstanbul

[Page 111]
Figure 20. Heat Mitigation Index of İstanbul City with the InVEST Model İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality

ADAPTATION ACTIONS
In general, the need to act for a rapid adaptation of the system is strictly necessary, considering that in the medium-term period, the scenarios can be even worse than the ones already predicted. 
Even if greenhouse gas emissions causing climate change is going to be hardly reduced today, the concentration of greenhouse gasses in the atmosphere will not be reduced to any significative effect, thus the effects of climate change will continue to impact the system. 
Adaptation is, therefore, necessary, which includes the capacity to alleviate the existing and expected risks while prioritizing the intervention on natural and anthropogenic systems with adequate models, techniques, and supports. As written in many recently published articles, the resilience of socio-economic systems and ecosystems needs to be improved in the light of knowledge of these vulnerabilities. These systems should be adopted where they are more vulnerable first.
Within the light of the scope presented above, adaptation for İstanbul will consist of systematic and coherently planned and designed application actions and policies, which should focus on minimizing the intensity of destructive impacts of potential hazards, while using a combined application of technological and nature-based solutions. These actions should keep in mind that in the long term, only the reduction of the anthropic footprint, in consumption, and in environmental degradation, can play an active role towards a more resilient system.
These actions include practical interventions such as improving existing infrastructure investments, planning new buildings, and the greening the city to adapt to the climate, but also include raising awareness and enhancing the preparedness of both institutions and people.
 
3.4.1. Methodology for Selection of Prioritized Adaptation Actions
A multistakeholder consultation approach which considers discussion of the topics by people from a variety of expert groups was utilized to make sure of the applicability and acceptability of potential adaptation actions. The experts from key institutions came together between 19-21 of September 2022 to discuss the possible adaptation options for each vulnerability and prioritize the most efficient and applicable ones based on their experiences. 
The meeting utilized 
Multi-Criteria-Analysis Methodology for determining and prioritizing the adaptation options for the list of adaptation actions. The results of the 
Sustainable Energy and Climate Action Plan of İstanbul

[Page 113]
consultation meetings are explained below.
 
3.4.2. Adaptation Actions for 
Each Vulnerability 
3.4.2.1. Urban Floods İstanbul is among the biggest 10 metropolitan areas in the world, in terms of population. 
Increased urbanization has led to changing or destruction of the natural systems, including the rivers and their basins in the city. After heavy precipitation, the riverbeds that are now a part of the urban areas are flooded causing both human and economic losses. 
The climate adaptation options indicated in this report are selected based on the circumstances of the parts of İstanbul that are most vulnerable to floods. However, the actual implementation stage may be subject to several bottlenecks. It may be challenging to revive the rivers and riverbeds back to their full functions, which will be extremely costly and would require strong cooperation among key institutions. Moreover, such activities may take many years to implement. Therefore, the following climate adaptation options presented in Table 8 are suggested, considering the capacities of the city to change and adapt. Still, adaptation options such as “Sponge Cities” may require some level of change to İstanbul’s infrastructure. 
These climate change adaptation actions are the ones selected for the most vulnerable districts of İstanbul, namely, Bağcılar, Esenyurt, Güngören, 
Bahçelievler, Gaziosmanpaşa. However, they can be implemented in all parts of the city, where natural habitats are destroyed, urbanization prevails over the natural environment, and there is high density of population. Especially districts of İstanbul such as: Beylikdüzü, Şişli, Kağıthane, Fatih, Bayrampaşa, 
Arnavutköy on the European side, and 
Sultanbeyli, Maltepe, Tuzla, Pendik,
