Permeable Pavements
Permeable paving surfaces are made of either a porous material that enables stormwater to flow through it, or nonporous blocks spaced so that water can flow between the gaps. Permeable paving can also include a variety of surfacing techniques for roads, parking lots, and pedestrian walkways. 
Permeable pavement surfaces may be composed of pervious concrete, porous asphalt, paving stones, or interlocking pavers. Unlike traditional impervious paving materials such as concrete and asphalt, permeable paving systems allow stormwater to percolate and infiltrate through the pavement and into the aggregate layers and/or soil below. In addition to reducing surface runoff, permeable paving systems can trap suspended solids, thereby filtering pollutants from stormwater.
Permeable pavement is commonly used on roads, paths and parking lots subject to light vehicular traffic, such as cycle-paths, service or emergency access lanes, road and airport shoulders, and residential sidewalks and driveways. Permeable pavement can be implemented in many zones in İstanbul, where it is not possible to relocate public that are at risk. 
Advantages:
•
Permeable paving surfaces are effective in managing runoff from paved surfaces and recharging groundwater aquifers. They can reduce downstream flooding and maintain base flows in rivers to keep ecosystems self-sustaining. 
•
They can control pollutants found in surface runoff, reducing suspended solids and harmful chemical concentrations within groundwater.
•
Permeable pavements enable improved water retention in urban environments, which are more vulnerable to high temperatures due to the heat island effect. Permeable paving, as well as green roofs, İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality

[Page 116]
maintain water within the local microecosystem, which keeps temperatures cooler when compared to a non-permeable paving system.
Disadvantages:
•
During the initial setup phase, it can be slightly more expensive than those made using traditional methods.
•
They may not be durable for a long time in places where heavy vehicles pass in the city. Therefore, they may not be suitable for airports and main roads.
•
Large storm events may cause the water table beneath the porous pavement to rise and prevent the precipitation from penetrating the ground. 
•
Special problems could arise in cold climates. Porous concrete pavements may be damaged due to wintertime salt treatment. 
•
Maintenance is necessary for permeable pavement systems, especially those with porous surfaces, to keep the pores free of fine particles and maintain the system's capacity to permit rainfall to permeate.
From an economic point of view, it can be difficult to compare cost impacts between conventional impervious surfaces and permeable surfaces given the variables such as: lifespan, geographic location, type of permeable paving system and site-specific factors. 
Therefore, the right type of pavement should be selected for İstanbul, and cost calculations should be made accordingly. 
Flood Planning and Management
Flood management or planning should adopt a holistic approach that incorporates human and socioeconomic issues as well as scientific and engineering challenges such as precipitation, runoff, and flooding. Flood protection and damage minimization are the goals of flood management plans. To mitigate the consequences of flooding in the city, preventive measures taken before the flood, as well as structural and non-structural measures taken during and after the flood, should be regarded as a whole and implemented by authorized institutions. These methods serve as the foundation for flood management. 
Flood 
Risk 
Pre-Assessment, Flood Hazard Maps, and Flood Risk Maps comprise the three-stage Flood Management Plan.
In İstanbul, 
Flood 
Risk 
Maps are conducted by State Hydraulic Works 
Sustainable Energy and Climate Action Plan of İstanbul

[Page 117]
(DSİ). At this point, the following actions are suggested as adaptation options:
•
Preparation of flood plans and managing floods in order to reduce the loss and damage in cooperation with DSİ
•
Integration of Flood Risk Maps into city planning and zone planning practices for all vulnerable hotspots in order to avoid losses in the future.
•
Cooperation with geomorphology experts while developing Flood Risk 
Maps49. Also, it would be suggested to involve the 
IMM in the map’s preparation processes are recommended for the DSİ. 
Five types of flood risk management strategies50 can be considered for İstanbul:
1. 
Flood risk prevention: Decreasing the exposure of people/property, etc., via methods that prohibit or discourage development in areas at risk of flooding (e.g., spatial planning, reallotment policy, expropriation policy, etc.). 
2. 
Flood defense: 
Aiming at decreasing the probability of flooding through infrastructural works, such as dikes, dams, embankments, and weirs, mostly referred to as “flood defense” or “structural measures” through measures that increase the capacity of the existing channels for water conveyance to increase space for water.
3. 
Flood risk mitigation: Focuses on decreasing the consequences of floods through measures inside the vulnerable area. They can be smart design of the flood-prone area, including spatial order, water retention within the protected area, or (regulations for) flood-proof building. 
4. 
Flood preparation: Consequences of floods can also be mitigated by preparing for a flood event. Measures include developing flood warning systems, preparing disaster management and evacuation plans, and managing a flood when it occurs.
5. 
Flood recovery: 
This strategy facilitates a good and fast recovery after a flood event. 
Measures include reconstruction or rebuilding plans as well as compensation or insurance systems.
Advantages:
• 
If flood planning is done on a Basin basis, it can be extremely viable.
• 
The organizations formed will serve as an excellent model for disaster preparedness in general.
İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality These maps are prepared by DSİ, therefore IMM is not addressed for this recommendation.
adopted from Hegger et al. 2014

[Page 118]
Disadvantages
It requires serious inter-agency cooperation. Some institutions may not have the necessary information about floods.
•
The measures may require significant financial resources.
•
There may be difficulties in finding personnel with technical and theoretical knowledge in the field.
Infiltration Trenches
Infiltration trenches are linear ditches that collect rainwater from adjacent surfaces, and their highly permeable soils allow the water to quickly seep into the ground. Because they are linear ditches, they are very practical to install parallel to roadsides, or around the perimeter of parking lots. 
Advantages:
•
The primary advantages of the infiltration trench are water quality treatment, reduction of peak flows in sewer systems, and groundwater recharge; so that the peak flow across the surface is reduced, and the amount of surface water entering waterways is slowed down. This reduces channel erosion. 
Disadvantages
•
Filtration systems such as this are prone to clogging with sediments suspended in the stormwater. 
Sponge City
Sponge 
Cities are, as their name suggests, designed to soak up as much extra water as possible. These areas are designed, or in many cases redesigned, to use a combination of storage tunnels, permeable pavements, rain gardens, constructed ponds and wetlands to store as much water as possible.
Sponge city has several interchangeable building blocks. 
At a large scale, protecting or restoring forests and natural ground cover helps give water a chance to sink in. At smaller scales, there are several options. Permeable pavement can be used on roadways, sidewalks, and pathways to allow water to infiltrate the ground, rather than wash off into the local stormwater system. 
Retention ponds and constructed wetlands help catch and filter water, allowing it to slowly percolate into the local water table. Rain gardens perform a similar function at a smaller scale and can easily be incorporated into neighborhood green space.
Sponge City indicates a particular type of city that allows any water to filter 
Sustainable Energy and Climate Action Plan of İstanbul

[Page 119]
through the ground, but more like a sponge, actually absorbing the rain water, which is then naturally filtered by the soil and allowed to reach into the urban aquifers. This allows for the extraction of water from the ground through urban or peri-urban wells. This water can be easily treated and used for the city water supply. 
Instead of causing floods, water can infiltrate through the soil, reducing the severeness and frequency of city floods, protecting lives and property. 
Moving towards implementation of sponge city, however requires infrastructural changes in the city. An abundance of spaces that allow water to seep through them, instead of only impermeable concrete and asphalt, is the primary need.
 The following applications are involved in sponge cities: 
•
Contiguous open green spaces, interconnected waterways, channels and ponds across neighborhoods that can naturally detain and filter water as well as foster urban ecosystems, boost biodiversity and create cultural and recreational opportunities.
•
Green roofs can retain rainwater and naturally filter it before it is recycled or released into the ground.
•
Porous design interventions across the city, including construction of bio-swales and bio-retention systems to detain run-off and allow for groundwater infiltration; porous roads and pavements, permeate and recharge groundwater; drainage systems that allow trickling of water into the ground or that direct storm water run-off into green spaces for natural absorption.
Advantages 
•
Reduction in flood risk as the city offers more permeable spaces for the natural retention and percolation of water. 
•
Replenished groundwater and thus greater water self-sufficiency
•
Cleaner groundwater due to the increased volume of naturally filtered storm water. 
•
Lower burdens on drainage systems, water treatment plants, artificial channels and natural streams. This also entails lower costs for drainage and treatment infrastructure.
•
Greener, healthier, more enjoyable urban spaces, improved quality of life, recreational areas, increased land values İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality

[Page 120]
Enriched biodiversity around green open spaces, wetlands, urban gardens and green rooftops
Disadvantages
•
Requirement for free spaces for sponge city applications
•
Financial requirements for implementation and maintenance of the systems
Green Roofs
Green roofs serve several purposes for a building, such as absorbing rainwater, providing insulation, creating a habitat for wildlife, and helping to lower urban air temperatures and mitigate the heat island effect.
Green roofs, which are also known as “vegetated roofs” or “living roofs”, consist of a waterproofing membrane, growing medium (soil) and vegetation (plants) overlying a traditional roof. 
Well-designed, engineered and maintained green roofs provide multiple environmental, social, economic, and aesthetic benefits.
Green roofs are suitable for retrofit or redevelopment projects, as well as for new buildings and can be installed on small garages or larger industrial, commercial and municipal buildings. 
They effectively use the natural functions of plants to filter water and treat air in urban and suburban landscapes.
With more than 1.5 million buildings in İstanbul, the city has a great potential to green its roofs, as a water retention measure to reduce the likeliness of floods. 
Advantages
•
Financial benefits that accompany green roofing, such as increased real estate value, and a reduction in energy use for heating and cooling. 
Green roofs allow buildings to better retain their heat during the cooler winter months while reflecting and absorbing solar radiation during the hotter summer months, allowing buildings to remain cooler.
•
Green roofing can extend the lifespan of a roof due to covering the roof with waterproofing membrane and with growing medium and vegetation, which shields the membrane from ultra-violet radiation and physical damage.
Disadvantages
•
The initial cost of installing a green roof can be higher than a normal roof.
Sustainable Energy and Climate Action Plan of İstanbul

[Page 121]
The additional mass of the soil substrate and retained water can cause a large pressure on the structural support of a building. This makes it unlikely for intensive green roofs to become widely implemented due to a lack of buildings that can support such a large amount of added weight as well as the added cost of reinforcing buildings to be able to support such weight. Some types of green roofs do have more demanding structural standards especially in seismic regions of the world. 
•
Green roofs may require significantly more maintenance and maintenance energy compared to a standard roof. 
Standard maintenance for green roofs includes removing debris, controlling weeds, deadhead trimming, checking moisture levels, and fertilizing. Maintenance costs depend on the type of green roof.
Considering the advantages and disadvantages of green roofs, İstanbul’s old building stock can be considered as less likely to host such roofs. However, the city is growing rapidly, and considerations can focus on new buildings, preferably on public buildings as pilot projects. 
3.4.2.2. Coastal floods İstanbul is surrounded by the Black Sea from the north and the Marmara Sea from the south. The Bosphorus connects the two seas51. The bathymetry map clearly shows that on both the Black Sea and Marmara Sea coasts, the sea depth increases sharply over short distances from the coast to the open sea. This circumstance, particularly in severe weather, will cause wave breaking to occur near to the coast, and the breaking waves will attack the coast quickly, causing coastal flooding as well as significant erosion. The fact that the prevailing wind direction in and around İstanbul is generally northeast-southwest and southwest-northeast will also strengthen this situation.
It should be noted that the whole of the floods occurring in the coastal part of the districts on the coast of the Marmara Sea are not related to the strong waves coming from the open sea. In fact, rivers flowing into the sea have either disappeared or had their natural flow paths considerably changed due to extensive urbanization in districts such as in Şişli, Avcılar, Bakırköy, Zeytinburnu, 
Kartal, Maltepe, Pendik, Göztepe, Kadıköy, 
Bostancı, and Eminönü. Particularly in these districts, the site-style residential areas have been transformed into some İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality Districts with coasts to the Sea of Marmara are: Silivri, Küçükçekmece and Büyükçekmece, Beylikdüzü, Avcılar, Bakırköy, Zeytinburnu, Kartal, 
Maltepe, Pendik, Göztepe, Kadıköy, Bostancı, Fatih. The districts bordering the 
Black Sea coast are the northern shores of: Çatalca, Arnavutköy, Eyüp, Sarıyer, 
Beykoz and Sarıyer districts' northern shores.

[Page 122]
small local artificial basins. The reinforced concrete constructions in these basins have completely destroyed the ground's permeability. Particularly after severe downpours, rainwater collected in such small basins rushes to the shore without having time to soak into the earth, causing floods in the coastal districts as the waves from the sea are forced onshore. The climate adaptation actions indicated below can significantly reduce coastal floods and coastal erosion for the parts of the city such as Kadıköy, Üsküdar, Fatih, 
Zeytinburnu, Kartal, which suffer from coastal floods from time to time. Coastal areas in these districts generally represent low and flat coastal characteristics, which increases their vulnerability to storm surges. 
The coasts in the specified coastal areas have low and flat coastal characteristics. 
Such low-lying coastal forms (like in 
Kadıköy, Üsküdar, Eminönü, and so on) are easily inundated by the sea as a result of sea swell, particularly during severe weather. If constructed in line with the defined adaption actions technique, the coastal structures defined will both considerably lessen the destructive effects of large waves on the shore in stormy weather and prevent the occurrence of events such as sea flooding on low-lying areas of the coastal zones. Additionally, these adaptation measures considerably minimize the rate and amount of erosion caused by waves and currents in coastal areas where they are applied, and they ensure that the shoreline is preserved. 
Thus, the chance of collapse or damage to the buildings located in the coastal area, especially the historical and aesthetic structures, is reduced.
These adaptation measures, particularly offshore breakwaters, can also greatly lessen the devastation caused by potential tsunami waves that can emerge from earthquakes that could occur in the 
Marmara and Black Sea regions.Although the above-mentioned adaptation activities were determined for the districts of 
Kadıköy, Üsküdar, 
Fatih,

Black Sea, Marmara, and Bosphorus. The

Adaptation options such as Seawall, 
Rip-rap, Gabion and shore stabilized structures can be constructed in order to minimize the erosion caused by the waves on the shore in almost all vulnerable districts. However, offshore breakwater constructions may result in certain unexpected sea traffic risks, particularly in districts with dense populations and considerable busy sea traffic. They can be implemented in the less dense and less populated districts located on the Black 
Sea coast.

Offshore Breakwater
An Offshore breakwater is an offshore parallel structure that breaks waves, reducing the wave energy reaching the beach and fostering sediment accretion between the beach and the breakwater. Made of rock, concrete, or oyster shell, these structures can be floating or fixed on the sea floor and can be continuous or segmented. Breakwaters structures can be placed attached to the shoreline as headlands or submerged near the shoreline as sills. Breakwaters reduce the intensity of wave action in inshore waters and thereby provide safe harborage. Breakwaters may also be small structures designed to protect a gently sloping beach to reduce coastal erosion; they are placed 30–90 m offshore in relatively shallow water. 
Advantages of offshore breakwaters are that they cause sand accumulation on the beach due to the decrease in wave energy. Due to these structures, waves break further offshore and therefore their erosive power is significantly reduced. Disadvantages of these structures are that they can be destroyed in severe storms and may be difficult to reinstall.
Offshore breakwater structures should be built in the districts located on the Marmara and Black Sea coasts to prevent coastal flooding and erosion that may occur on the coast due to strong waves coming from the open sea during storms.
However, the application of offshore breakwater structures may not be appropriate, especially in districts that have a coast on the Bosphorus (Fatih, Üsküdar, Beşiktaş, 
Beyoğlu, Beykoz, Sarıyer coasts). Because the Bosphorus is one of the areas in the world with its great maritime traffic and such structures may pose significant hazards to maritime traffic in the Bosphorus. Since the Bosphorus does not have a coast to open seas, there are strong currents rather than strong waves here. These strong strait currents only cause erosion rather than coastal flooding on the coasts.
As these structures require offshore construction, a feasibility needs to be made with care for their costs and benefits. Also, an assessment of acceptability can be conducted with the local residents. 
Sustainable Energy and Climate Action Plan of İstanbul

[Page 125]
Seawalls
A seawall is a type of coastal defense built where the sea and associated coastal processes have a direct impact on the landforms of the shore. A seawall's objective is to protect areas of human residence, conservation, and recreational activities from the damaging impact of waves. Because a seawall is a static feature, it will interfere with the dynamic nature of the shoreline and limit sediment exchange between land and sea. 
These structures are already in place in many parts of İstanbul for many years. 
Advantages
•
They provide great defense in areas with significant wave energy.
•
During storms, they protect the coast from destructive wave impacts.
•
They have a lengthy lifespan.
•
They divert hazardous and destructive waves to other locations.
Disadvantages
•
Scours form at the bottom of eroding beaches, disturbing the natural balance of the beach.
•
Must be constructed along the entire coastline; otherwise, the nearby shoreline will erode.
•
They can be quite expensive to build.
•
They produce an unsightly appearance on the beach.
•
It can make access to beaches difficult.
İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality

[Page 126]
Gabions also protect beaches from waves and current erosion. 
They resemble baskets packed with crushed rock or stones composed of cobbles or wire mesh. They are typically filled on-site with materials that are easily available in the area, making them potentially inexpensive. 
In close proximity to inclined "mattresses" or vertical cubic baskets, gabions can be used. 
Secondly, it is often not appropriate for usage on all kinds of shorelines, and they are built for stabilizing cliffs or the beach.
Advantages
•
Cheaper than other forms of coastal defense
•
Rock cages absorb wave energy
•
As they are flexible and porous, they can absorb wave energy, reducing the scour problems associated with impermeable man-made sea structures such as concrete seawalls.
•
Gabions offer an easy-to-use method of reducing water velocity on to the shore and protecting slopes from erosion.
Disadvantages
•
Wire cages can break, and they need to be securely tied down securely
•
They do not offer a good view of the coast.
•
They are vulnerable to very strong waves.
•
Difficult to install, generally requires large equipment.
Rip-raps
Rip-rap, also known as, rock armour or rubble, is human-placed rock or other material used to protect shoreline structures against scour and highly destructive wave impact. Rip-raps are used to armour shorelines, streambeds, bridge abutments, foundational infrastructure supports, and other shoreline structures against erosion.
Advantages
•
They are relatively cost-efficient compared to other adaptation actions in terms of cost. 
•
They reduce the destructive effect of strong waves and currents on the coast and significantly slow down erosion.
Sustainable Energy and Climate Action Plan of İstanbul

[Page 127]
They prevent flooding in the coastal area.
Disadvantages
•
They make the coastline look unsightly.
•
They impede and can endanger people's access to the beach.
Like any other project which requires construction, rip-raps also come with a cost which need to be considered through a cost-benefit analysis. Also, the acceptability of such structures needs to be assessed among local residents. 
Cliff Stabilization 
Cliffs can fail due to several different factors, such as their composition or rock type, geological structure (faults, joints, etc.), climate and wave energy. 
There are soft and loose cliffs made up of sand, silt, clay, marl or chalk which are more prone to erosion and landslides, and hard cliffs made up of limestone, granite or sandstone. 
Erosion of cliffs in coastal areas results in the retreat of the coastline because the amount of material which is eroded exceeds that which is deposited. 
Cliff stabilization techniques are applied to reduce the amount of erosion, landslide, collapse and rockfall potential of the cliffs. One of the cliff stabilization techniques can be terracing the steepness of the cliff using various engineering techniques or increasing the stability of the cliff by planting plants on or on the cliff slope. 
Planting vegetation strengthens the structure of the cliff and increases the adhesion of the material forming the cliff to each other, thereby preventing landslides, slips, etc. prevent it from collapsing for reasons such as: Another method is to drain the excess water in the cliff. This practice prevents water from seeping through weak surfaces (cracks) in the cliff, thus preventing the rocks and sediment from slipping or falling to the bottom of the cliff along these weak surfaces. This will also limit the possibility of liquefaction which can cause sludge flows. In İstanbul, this harmonization measure can be applied to protect coastlines with eroded cliffs.
Advantages
•
Reduces risk of injury to beach users by slumping or sliding of cliff material. 
•
Enables the development of buildings and facilities on the cliff top with a reduced risk of collapse and failure. İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality

[Page 128]
stability of the cliffs will encourage tourism in coastal resorts and will have economic benefits for the area. 
•
Planting vegetation on cliff tops will increase the biodiversity of the area as it will act as a food source or habitat for wildlife. 
•
It slows the occurrence of landslides in the coast's cliffs (steep slopes).
•
It reduces the rate of the cliff's retreat.
Disadvantages
•
Vegetation growth is not immediate and will take time - therefore this may be more of a medium-long term solution. 
Sustainable Energy and Climate Action Plan of İstanbul 126

[Page 129]
3.4.2.3. Habitat Degradation
Cities cause erosion of biodiversity all around the world. Urban expansion leads to soil sealing and destruction of habitats, and the consumption patterns of habitants cause pollution and environmental degradation. İstanbul is no different than other metropolitan areas in the world when it comes to threats to its biodiversity. 
Rapid urbanization, which rarely considers natural assets combined with increasing pollution, and population threaten all natural habitats of the city. 
Trees play an important role in reducing pollution in cities. Green infrastructure can cool down the temperature around it. Planting noise buffers made of trees and shrubs has proven to be an effective method to reduce urban noise. 
Also, connection to nature is proven to be beneficial for mental health. 
Planting trees can prevent landslides and flooding, and green infrastructure can also be used to manage rainwaters flows and enhance groundwater reserves. Therefore, loss of biodiversity will mean that we may suffer from several consequences in cities.
Cities do not have a homogenous distribution of urbanization and natural habitat zones. In İstanbul, there are parts where urbanization prevails over natural habitats in highly populated areas, as well as the parts that are mostly covered with a natural environment. And finally, there are border areas between highly urbanized areas and habitat zones. 
As vulnerability studies show us, the most endangered habitat zones are the border areas where urban zones expand towards the habitat zones. A variety of climate adaptation options have been selected below in Table 10 for the 3 zones indicated. However, it should be noted that the majority of biodiversity loss happens due to urbanization and pollution, rather than from climate change. Climate change exacerbates biodiversity loss with the impact of urbanization and associated pollution.

Enhancing 
Cooperation with 
Local 
Actors on Biodiversity Loss
Local governments’ cooperation with local actors with all kinds of semi-public and private actors in the local area for the protection of biodiversity and to raise awareness in the society is very important. 
IMM and district municipalities have access and potential communication opportunities with local actors such as local businesses, NGOs, academia, as well as residents in neighborhoods. Enhanced cooperation with such actors for preserving biodiversity can support IMM and district municipalities to increase the effectiveness of their actions. 
An example is cooperation regarding data gathering with NGOs and academia on health and/or quantity of particular species in the city and its towns. Similarly monitoring activities regarding the impacts of actions of the IMM can be supported by relevant local actors. Such activities can be discussed, agreed and mainstreamed through multistakeholder events in the city. 
Like many other “environmental measures” this climate adaptation option will include cooperation, communication activities with local stakeholders through workshops and events. They may also include activities that include more public engagement such as public surveys. 
Therefore, such a measure is expected to have high acceptability by the public. 
The bottlenecks of this action may involve increased efforts and communication for engaging with a variety of stakeholders which need to be coordinated carefully. 
Participation in National Efforts for 
Preventing Biodiversity Loss
Local governments have a unique role in public governance through their mandate of serving residents in cooperation with national institutes that are represented locally. This enables the local governments to have local solutions with the help of national institutions. Just like for many other responsibilities that the local governments have, preventing biodiversity loss exacerbated by climate change can only be done with the cooperation of local governments with national institutions. 
This climate adaptation option is considered as a “soft measure” as it does not particularly involve “tangible” actions such as installations or construction. İstanbul 
Metropolitan 
Municipality’s potential cooperation with national efforts to prevent biodiversity loss can be implemented through participation to İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality

[Page 132]
implementation of national policies and measures that are implemented in 
Türkiye. Possible synergies between national efforts and IMM’s efforts to prevent biodiversity loss can within the framework of national policies and measures in the subject, such as 
Türkiye’s 
National 
Climate 
Change 
Adaptation Strategy and Action Plan53 (2010-2023), and the National Action 
Plan for Biodiversity54 (2018-2028). 
There are several points in these documents where local governments are indicated to have responsibilities. 
Examples are monitoring the areas which are converted from forest areas to urban areas, implementing all activities on coastal zones in a manner to give the least harm to coastal ecosystems. Such activities require cooperation with the 
Ministry of 
Environment, 
Urbanization and

Ministry of Agriculture and Forestry and their local branches in İstanbul. 
Combining the national capacities of the ministries with the local capacities of 
IMM can create synergies in developing cooperation with the local branches of the line ministries of the Metropolitan 
Municipality. Collaboration areas may include (but may be limited to) a variety of activities such as data collection, planning, strategy development and project development. 
Such measures including enhanced cooperation among state institutions always have high levels of acceptability by the public. However, they come with bottlenecks of potential risks around cooperation and coordination and working cultures and the mandates of each institution. https://webdosya.csb.gov.tr/db/iklim/editordosya/file/eylem%20planlari/uyum_stratejisi_eylem_plani_EN_Final.pdf
Sustainable Energy and Climate Action Plan of İstanbul

[Page 133]
Capacity Building in Local Governments
Knowledge on climate change and the prevention of biodiversity loss are key topics which need to be known not only by the Municipal department that are dealing with the issue daily. It is not enough that only the strategy developers or planners work on it. Staff with a variety of roles such as city planners, zone planners, field workers and even cleaners should at least have the minimum understanding on these key topics, so that they can continue their work considering climate and biodiversity in their daily actions. 
Capacity building activities can include several topics besides climate change itself, such as ecosystem services, natural conservation, protection of biodiversity, etc. combined with how they can integrate them in their work. 
There are no particular bottlenecks for capacity building activities, and a high level of acceptability among the public can be expected. What needs to be considered for increased engagement is a clear explanation of why these activities are organized and how participants can benefit from them. 
Restoration and Rehabilitation in Urban 
Zones
Degraded ecosystems in cities are mainly the areas where urbanization overcomes the natural habitats. 
Environmental conditions and species composition of most urban ecosystems are highly modified. Due to the lack of (semi-) natural reference systems available for defining restoration targets and assessing restoration success in urban areas, it is difficult to design and implement ecological restoration in cities. However, this is exactly where local non-state actors can step in and support the IMM to define and implement biodiversity restoration targets in İstanbul and monitor progress. 
Examples of restoration and rehabilitation of biodiversity includes the introduction of native plant species, allowing regrowth of natural bank vegetation, development of wild urban woodlands with native species. Urban restoration approaches must consider local history and site conditions, as well as citizens’ needs. 
From the acceptability point of view, it is important that each restoration and rehabilitation action has some level of acceptability by the residents of İstanbul. From an economic point of İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality

[Page 134]
view, restoration and rehabilitation activities can be affordable or expensive depending on the choices of methods. 
It should also include integrating practices and making choices in a way to preserve the ecosystem, rather than taking new measures which are new to 
Municipality’s activities, which can reduce the potential costs of implementing a new measure. 
There are no particular disadvantages of this climate adaptation measure, as long as it is implemented with general consent in a way to prevent biodiversity loss and contribute to restoration and rehabilitation of the city’s biodiversity. 
Monitoring and Control in Natural 
Habitat Areas
Areas where natural habitat areas are relatively better protected in İstanbul are mainly natural parks and other protected areas. Some of the city parks are under the jurisdiction of the IMM, while the protected areas are under the jurisdiction of state institutions at national level such as the Ministry of Agriculture and 
Forestry. This situation gives limited opportunities to the IMM to plan and implement measures to protect biodiversity in İstanbul. However, the IMM can take measures in the areas under its control, such as city parks, through monitoring the health of habitats, implemented activities for habitat protection and their results. 
Data gathering and monitoring are important activities for management of any system, and biodiversity is no different. For this action, the IMM can seek support from academia and NGOs as needed.
This activity is already being implemented partially by the IMM. The extent of monitoring and stakeholder engagement can be enhanced in order to increase the efficiency and effectiveness of the measure. A roadmap can be developed for building upon existing systems instead of developing a new mechanism, which will ensure cost saving for this action. 
Sustainable Energy and Climate Action Plan of İstanbul

[Page 135]
The advantages of this adaptation are that it can enhance cooperation between the IMM and non-state actors; it increases public engagement; increases the effectiveness and efficiency of the 
IMM’s existing actions preventing biodiversity loss. There is no particular disadvantage to this adaptation action. 
Integrating Biodiversity Conservation in all Municipal Activities
Biodiversity conservation is a cross-cutting adaptation option that can be integrated in several activities of the Municipality. 
This mainly includes all physical activities that are or are not related to climate change, nor nature conservation. 
Examples include actions such as choosing pesticides that do not give harm to birds and other animals than the pests. Other examples include caring for the bird nests when pruning off the trees. 
The advantages of this adaptation option is that it can have direct positive impacts on the existing biodiversity in highly urbanized areas. Also, this measure is a soft measure, that does not require physical actions such as construction or implementation, but rather requires changes in planning and approach to implementation of the existing activities of the Municipalities. 
However, there is a need to change the practices of the Municipality in all actions that directly or indirectly have an impact on the nature. That may require a range of plans and practices to change, including documentation such as technical specifications of purchased material for the activities. 
Therefore, thorough planning is required for implementing this measure. 
Green Spaces İstanbul is a continuously growing city both physically, economically, and population-wise. Inevitably, urban zones are growing into natural habitats, causing pollution and biodiversity loss. However, it is possible to protect the biodiversity to some extent through leaving “green spaces” when making zoning plans, spatial plans and other planning for the new urban areas. 
Urban green spaces can be defined as outdoor places with significant amounts of vegetation and exist mainly as semi-natural areas. Urban areas can contain rich flora that contribute significantly to biodiversity, but loss and isolation of habitats due to urban sprawl threaten biodiversity and warrant limits on development. The connectivity provided by urban green spaces offers habitats and corridors that help to conserve biodiversity. Many cities develop İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality

[Page 136]
green space networks and increase connectivity to preserve and restore biodiversity. 
In sustainable urban development, urban greening is a key element, but biodiversity must be an integral component of this greening. Consequently, preserving habitat and dispersal routes and developing a green space network that can maintain landscape-scale connectivity is crucial in urban biodiversity conservation.
The development of urban green space networks includes the protection of existing green spaces, creation of new spatial forms, and restoration and maintenance of connectivity among diverse green spaces.
Zoning plans is a critical area where the 
IMM has the opportunity to make decisions in a way to protect the biodiversity in İstanbul. Making the zoning plans in a way to leave enough natural habitat areas to avoid biodiversity loss is the essence of this climate adaptation action. Green space networks can provide a solution to the problems of intensified land use and fragmentation, enabling natural populations of species and threatened habitats to survive. The EU’s 2030 biodiversity strategy encourages bringing nature back into cities by creating biodiverse and accessible green infrastructure.
Sustainable Energy and Climate Action Plan of İstanbul

[Page 137]
3.4.2.4. Heat Islands
Heat islands contribute to higher daytime temperatures, reduced night-time cooling, and higher air-pollution levels. These, in turn, contribute to heat-related deaths and heat-related illnesses such as general discomfort, respiratory difficulties, heat cramps, heat exhaustion, and non-fatal heat stroke.
Heat islands can also exacerbate the impact of naturally occurring heat waves, which are periods of abnormally hot, and often humid, weather. 
Sensitive populations are particularly at risk during these events.
Older adults are among the most vulnerable to extreme heat events. 
Many physiological, psychological, and socioeconomic factors contribute to this danger. Older adults are more likely to be in poor health, to be less mobile and more isolated, to be more sensitive to high heat. People in poor health, including people with chronic conditions, disabilities, mobility constraints, and those taking certain medications, are vulnerable to extreme temperatures. İstanbul hosts a significant elderly population who should be careful in extreme heat conditions. 
Populations with low-income are at greater risk of heat-related illnesses due to poor housing conditions, including lack of air conditioning and small living spaces, and inadequate resources to find alternative shelter during a heat wave. 
People who spend their working hours outdoors are more prone to conditions such as heat exhaustion and heat stroke. They have higher exposures to ozone air pollution and heat stress, especially if work tasks involve heavy exertion. A significant population’s occupation requires them to work outdoors in İstanbul. Therefore, it is important to implement adaptation options (Table 11) at least in the hotspots of the city. 
There are no particular districts that suffer more than the others in İstanbul city. However, almost every town has its “hotspots” which are mainly the town center, that needs an intervention. İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality

[Page 138]

[Page 139]
From Grey to Green İstanbul, being a big metropolitan area, has several grey spaces such as squares and other public areas which are mostly covered with cement. Changing the cement areas with “green” ones by extending plant areas with planting trees and other plant species which do not require much water. 
Trees significantly reduce the temperatures in their shadow, creating a cool ambient for the city. Other plant species on the ground can absorb the heat and radiation from the sun preventing the heat island effect at their local environment. 
Other advantages of greening the green areas besides reducing the ambient temperatures and heat island effect is to increase public health by reducing the impact of increased temperatures and heat waves. Also, green areas provide recreational areas within the city, increasing the life quality of the public. 
Also changing public areas from grey to green provides additional green zones which can support increased connectivity and decrease fragmentation between green spaces, to preserve biodiversity. 
This climate adaptation option is significantly beneficial for all İstanbul and its grey spaces due to low investment cost and high potential immediate impacts. İstanbul can embrace this option though changing several big grey public areas, into greener areas in a manner not reducing public access to the spaces. 
What needs to be taken care is that the types of plants and trees should be selected among the ones which do not need much maintenance and irrigation, considering water scarcity issues in the city. 
No particular disadvantage of this adaptation option is considered for İstanbul. Therefore, this option can bring positive results in all regions of İstanbul. Naturally from an economic point of view, application of this adaptation measure in highly populated areas can be beneficial. And from a public health point of view, this measure can be applied focusing on regions where socio-economic development is relatively low in order to create resilience against heat waves for elderly people.
This measure is expected to be highly welcomed by the public, thus having increased acceptability. Therefore, a major bottleneck in its implementation is not expected. İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality

[Page 140]
Shades in the City İstanbul has so many open spaces where the ground is cement or asphalt, which exacerbates the heat island effect and increases the local temperatures, causing public health problems. Providing shade in the city at locations where possible can significantly reduce local temperatures creating cooler spaces for people. Also, the cost of cooling shops and closed areas may be significantly decreased. 
This measure is already applied by people in places like marketplaces and open space shopping malls successfully for many years. Similar applications can be done at places where greening grey areas are not feasible. Examples are narrow streets where there is high pedestrian traffic, such as İstiklal 
Street or Kadıköy Bahariye Street. In such locations, canopies or shades made of a variety of materials such as textile or hard materials can be implemented for the summer season. 
The installation of these materials will not require construction, which may be costly. It only requires the installation of systems that can be easily dismantled in the off-season. 
The only downside of these systems might be the requirement of installation at the beginning of summer and disassembling at the end of summer. 
Implementation of this adaptation measure is regardless of the towns of İstanbul, as the heat island effect is observed in all highly populated areas of the city. The measure can be considered for the most populated parts of each town with high pedestrian traffic. 
No disadvantage of this adaptation option is considered for İstanbul. 
Therefore, this option can bring positive results in all regions of İstanbul. And from a public health point of view, this measure can be applied focusing on regions where socio-economic development is relatively low in order to create resilience against heat waves for elderly people.
This measure is expected to be highly welcomed by the public, thus having increased acceptability. Therefore, a major bottleneck in its implementation is not expected. 
Sustainable Energy and Climate Action Plan of İstanbul

[Page 141]
Green Infrastructure İstanbul is a dynamic and growing city with constant need of maintenance and modifications in the built environment. 
Investing in green infrastructure can address several societal challenges such as water security and public health while protecting nature in cities. 
Green infrastructures are mainly “nature-based solutions” which have been highly in focus over the recent years in the international fight against climate change.
Green infrastructure includes several applications, such as open storm water systems, which store access water in water canals and ponds in case of heavy rain, where water is later on used for irrigation of green areas in the city. 
Another example is integrating wide areas made of cement or asphalt with the canals, so the water gathers in the canals without causing inland floods. 
Also, such examples include changing material on the ground from asphalt to permeable cement (with holes, for example) providing space for water to reach the ground, without compromising functionality. Such an application cannot be done on roads, but they can be easily applied to parking areas. 
Green infrastructure is in principle extending the green and blue areas of the city through integrating nature-based solutions into the infrastructure and built environment. 
Because of the dynamic nature of İstanbul’s built environment and urbanization, integrating green infrastructure in existing process may have short-term results in neighborhoods. 
As the heat island effect is not focused on particular towns, but rather in hot spots where there is high urbanization, therefore this adaptation measure is not limited to any particular towns of İstanbul. 
However, there may be some barriers to implementation of green infrastructure. 
These are mainly due to the already densely populated and tightly built nature of İstanbul. In many parts of the city, the existing built environment may not allow water canals or water ponds. 
However, a careful design considering the existing circumstances in neighborhoods may help to overcome such barriers. This will require case by case design of such systems, customized for each town/area.
İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality

[Page 142]
Light Colors in Streets, Pavements and 
Apart from the adaptation options that may require changes in the infrastructure, application of light colors in the built environment to the extent possible is a low-cost alternative. It is simply utilizing light colors in streets, pavements and roofs which can reflect light back to the atmosphere, providing some level of albedo effect in the cities. 
This adaptation measure can be distinguished from others with its low-cost nature. However, there is not data available as to how much it can support reducing temperatures in hot spots in the cities especially when they are used on the ground. They may have a different impact on the roofs, as they have potential of reducing cooling costs for buildings in summer. However similarly, they may tend to reduce the heating effect of the sun during wintertime. Therefore, the choice of implementing this measure should be well thought and considered. 
There is no bottleneck in terms of applying this measure in İstanbul. 
However, the willingness of residents needs to be assessed before implementing this measure, like many others. 
Green roofs and walls İstanbul, with its population which exceeds 16 million people, and with more than 1.5 million buildings inevitably suffers from the heat island effect. And reducing the heat island effect involves interventions to buildings to the extent possible. 
To create green surfaces on existing and new roofs and walls is a way to reclaim nature and at the same time achieve other benefits. A green roof is created by sowing or planting directly on a layer of soil on the roofs. 
Green roofs and walls have a direct reducing impact on the heat island effect in cities, as the green surfaces absorb the heat and radiation from the sun unlike many artificial surfaces that are widely used in cities. Besides supporting the city with the reduction of the heat island effect, green roofs can provide insulation for the buildings, reducing heating and cooling costs, support biodiversity conservation in the cities, help rainwater harvesting, support preventing city floods and also sequester carbon. Similarly, green walls can reduce the reflection of radiation from walls, significantly contributing to reduced local temperatures in cities. 
Also, green roofs have longer lifetimes compared to conventional roofs. 
Sustainable Energy and Climate Action Plan of İstanbul

[Page 143]
Despite the variety of positive impacts that the green roofs and walls can bring, depending on the type of green roof used, they can be high-maintenance structures. Therefore, comprehensive cost and benefit calculations should be made before planning such structures. 
Several parameters will be involved in such calculations, such as construction cost, maintenance cost per year, energy savings, water retention, etc. It is possible to say that such structures could be more cost efficient in buildings with wide rooftop areas. Currently, as there are not so many examples of green roofs in Türkiye, it is difficult to make benchmarking and make decisions. 
However, pilot cases implemented by public institutions such as İstanbul 
Metropolitan Municipality can become a role model for future private investments. This is a way of promoting private climate financing for the whole city and perhaps for the other metropolitans in Türkiye.
Like the other climate adaptation options for heat islands, this measure can be implemented anywhere in the city. However, the effectiveness would be higher in densely urbanized parts of the city. This measure is expected to be highly welcomed by the public, thus having increased acceptability. 
Therefore, a major bottleneck in its implementation is not expected. İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality

[Page 144]
Sustainable Energy and Climate Action Plan of İstanbul

All of the problems arising from the vulnerability assessments may not be immediately resolved throughout İstanbul due to technical and budgetary constraints. Therefore, the climate extremes, system vulnerability and the exposure of İstanbul, as explained in 
Climate Change Action Report were studied to determine the city vulnerability maps. 
The identification of the regions through these maps, where climate change impacts are concentrated, pointed out vulnerability hotspots. Based on these hotspots, the potentially applicable adaptation actions were identified, which were then narrowed down to the list of adaptation actions as per city's specificities. 4. IMM BUILDING ENERGY 
ACTIONS AND MITIGATION PLANS

[Page 149]
INTRODUCTION TO STATIONARY 
SOURCES AND BUILDINGS
Through 
SECAP, energy efficiency measures are targeted at the sectors with the greatest potential for energy savings, such as the building, industry, transport, and waste sectors. 
By implementing energy efficiency measures in buildings, citizens can reap a multitude of benefits. Lower energy bills, improved quality of life, reduced reliance on external suppliers of oil and gas, and support for the sustainable growth of the economy are just some of the advantages. Although there may be costs associated with renovations, the benefits of energy savings far outweigh them.
Renovating both public and private buildings is an essential action and has been singled out in the European Green 
Deal as a key initiative to drive energy efficiency in the sector and deliver on objectives. 
A long-term renovation strategy to support the renovation of buildings into highly energy efficient and decarbonised buildings stock by 2050 is also essential to meet Türkiye’s 2053 zero-emission target. Making buildings more energy efficient will also contribute significantly to İstanbul city in achieving its energy and climate goals. In this regard, renovating the IMM buildings will improve energy efficiency while driving the clean energy transition for the whole city.
This section describes the IMM building stocks and energy efficiency activities that will be used as the basis for making the investments in the renovation of IMM buildings, its affiliates and companies and insights for private residential buildings in İstanbul in order to achieve energy efficiency to the relevant extent.
4.2. COMPLIANCE OF THE ENERGY 
SAVING 
ACTION 
PLANS 
FOR 
BUILDINGS and 
FACILITIES 
WITH 
NATIONAL POLICY DOCUMENTS 
Considering the future consumption projections made by the International 
Energy 
Agency (IEA) considering technological developments, economic growth rate, population growth rate, energy prices, continued energy policies and consumer movements, it is projected that world energy consumption will increase by 40% on average until 2035. 
A series of measures have been taken and policies have been determined to combat climate change and protect the global climate system. Some countries, especially Türkiye, have become party to the United Nations Framework 
Convention on Climate Change and the 
Kyoto Protocol, which tackle climate İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality

[Page 150]
Sustainable Energy and Climate Action Plan of İstanbul change mitigation measures. Türkiye, which is a candidate country in the EU membership process, continues to work on aligning with the policies implemented by the EU member states to comply with the EU acquis. In this context, studies are carried out in line with the directives to successfully complete the Environment chapter, which is among the most important chapters in the EU harmonization process. The National Energy Action 
Plan 2024-2030 was prepared and submitted to the European Union 
Commission. 
Incentives have been provided in order to increase the use of renewable energy resources in Türkiye during the harmonization process.
In addition, The Ministry of Energy and 
Natural Resources' Strategy Plan for 
2017-2023 has set a goal of achieving a 
15% electricity saving by 2023, the 
100th year of the Republic. In the national 11th Development Plan, it is foreseen to encourage the necessary planning and investments in order to increase the energy efficiency in existing buildings, to establish the National 
Green Building Certification System and to expand unlicensed solar energy investments to meet own electricity needs.
The following laws and regulations are the backbone of national energy efficiency legislation:
•

In addition, the Government of Türkiye has taken steps to ensure energy efficiency with the publication of the “Energy Efficiency Strategy Document 
2012-2023” on February 25, 2012 and the “National Energy Efficiency Action

[Page 151]
Plan 2017-2023” on January 2, 2018. 
These documents set out clear targets, methods and implementation plans to ensure the country's energy efficiency.
On the other hand, the Presidential 
Circular on "15% Energy Savings in the 
Public" published on August 16, 2019, and the "Energy 
Performance 
Agreements in the Public Service" published on August 21, 2020, have both been instrumental in driving energy efficiency in the public sector. These two initiatives have provided the necessary guidance and support to public sector entities to identify and implement energy-saving measures, resulting in significant cost savings and environmental benefits.
Regulation on Increasing Efficiency in the Use of Energy Resources and 
Energy (Energy 
Efficiency 
Regulation): 
It has been prepared for the implementation of the Energy Efficiency 
Law No. 5627, which consists of 39 articles, 15 temporary articles, and has been amended 4 times, the last of which was on March 14, 2020. 
Energy 
Performance 
Regulation in 
Buildings (BEP Regulation):
It is published in order to regulate the procedures and principles regarding the effective and efficient use of energy and energy resources in buildings, the prevention of energy waste and the protection of the environment and is applied in new buildings and important renovations of existing buildings that require project change. With the BEP 
Regulation, the following topics are regulated: architectural, mechanical and electrical projects of buildings, thermal insulation, mechanical installation, heating and cooling system, ventilation and air conditioning systems, sanitary hot water preparation and distribution systems, automatic control, electrical installation and lighting systems, renewable energy sources, heat pump and cogeneration systems design and implementation, operation, periodical maintenance and inspection, and Energy Identity Certificate. For the buildings to be built to obtain a "building occupancy permit", their projects and construction must comply with at least 
C class defined in the regulation. 
The concept of “Nearly Zero Energy 
Building (NZEB)” was introduced to the legislation by the 
Ministry of 
Environment, Urbanization and Climate 
Change. The aim was to create buildings with high energy performance that incorporate a certain amount of İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality

[Page 152]
Sustainable Energy and Climate Action Plan of İstanbul renewable energy sources. With the regulation, as of January 1, 2025, all buildings with a total construction area of more than 2,000 m2 must be constructed according to 
NZEB principles and at least 10% of the primary energy needs of these buildings must be met from renewable energy sources. In addition, NZEB buildings shall have energy performance class B or better in the Energy Performance 
Certificate. However, with the two temporary articles added to the regulation, stipulating that the building construction area required for NZEB projects would be 5,000 m2 between 
January 1, 2023 and January 1, 2025, and the renewable energy usage rate would be 5%. 
Public buildings with an annual total energy consumption of 250 TEP or a total construction area of more than 
10,000 m2 are considered "obligatory" and this responsibility is given to the "building management". If there is more than one independent building in a building group or campus, the sum of the construction areas of the individual buildings is considered as the total construction area. 
The management of public sector buildings with a total construction area of at least 10,000 m2 or an annual total energy consumption of 250 TOE or more, assigns an employee with an energy manager certificate among the employees in their buildings as an energy manager (with the exceptions specified in the 36th article of the relevant Regulation). TS EN ISO 50001 
Energy Management System must be established and documented in obliged buildings until the end of 2023. Relevant institutions, organizations and businesses are responsible for keeping the energy management system up to date. It is necessary to have an audit done every seven years in the obliged buildings. 
Presidential 
Circular dated 
August 
2019 and numbered 
2019/18: 
According to the Presidential Circular, a minimum of 15% energy efficiency until the end of 2023 in public buildings, with an annual total energy consumption of 250 TOE or more, or a total construction area of 10,000 m2 or more, shall be achieved. 
By the end of 2023, savings less than 
14% are classified as “unsuccessful”, savings between 14% and 16% are classified as “successful”, and savings more than 16% are classified as “super successful”. The unsuccessful ones shall submit their reasoned reports to the Ministry until the end of 2024.

4.3.2. Energy Action Plans of İstanbul Buildings, Houses and IMM Affiliates/Subsidiaries İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality (IMM) has limited authority to impose regulations on buildings in İstanbul City to meet its 2030 carbon reduction targets. All residential, commercial, and industrial structures, including IMM's own buildings and facilities, are subject to the control and legislation established by the central government. As such, 
IMM is obliged to make its own buildings and facilities energy efficient, increasing renewable energy production, and constructing its new buildings that are nearly zero-emissions. Promoting usage of renewables and energy efficiency in residential buildings and assisting district municipalities to create building permits that adhere to energy efficiency standards are key actions that can be taken by IMM to help reduce energy consumption. By encouraging the public to use energy-saving practices and providing guidance to district municipalities on how to create energy-efficient building permits, IMM can help to reduce energy consumption and create a more sustainable future.
Within this context, IMM has determined the following actions and reductions in the İstanbul Climate Action Plan, as summarized in Table 19 and Table 20. These tables have been updated within the scope of 2023 legislation and developments.

4.4.1. Energy Consumption and Targets Outlook
In 2019, İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality (IMM) conducted an inventory of greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions and energy consumption in İstanbul City, including corporate GHG emissions and corporate energy consumption of stationary energy sources (such as IMM institutional buildings and facilities). This baseline inventory serves as a reference point for future GHG emissions and energy consumption reduction efforts. Table 22 provides information on GHG emissions from IMM institutional stationary sources.
IMM has created the GHG inventory as an institutional energy consumption inventory, which includes municipality administrative buildings and their activities and as well relevant subsidiaries/affiliate companies’ activities.

[Page 165]
The analysis of energy consumption at the corporate level for local government activities includes the quantification of 
Greenhouse Gas (GHG) emissions from all major organizations and services. 
This includes emissions resulting from energy consumption activities and activities where local government institutions have operational control, regardless of the location of the emissions.
IMM is responsible for public transportation, waste management, water supply, sewerage system and treatment, recreational areas, road paving, urban and zone planning. IMM is not involved in the production, transmission, distribution of electricity, or public street lighting for the city. 
However, it does operate a few energy production plants, such as solar and waste-to-energy plants, to generate electricity for its own use.
As of 2023, IMM has 30 subsidiaries and 
2 affiliated companies. However, in 
2019, which was taken as a basis for the emission inventory, 
IMM had subsidiaries and 2 affiliated companies. 
During the energy consumption assessment process, data on energy consumption was collected from these companies. From this data, seven companies were identified as having the most significant impact on energy consumption and as having energy efficiency plans and targets for energy-using and emission-emitting activities. These companies are İETT, İSTAÇ, İSTON, İSFALT, İSKİ, İHE and 
METRO İSTANBUL.

In order to meet their ambitious goals by 
2030, the seven companies have conducted extensive long-term research to identify the necessary actions. These include reducing energy consumption through efficiency investments and increasing renewable energy investments. 
The specifics of these actions are outlined in Annex 1 in detail. Apart from these targeted and planned actions, other energy efficiency and renewable energy investments may also be required for 40% reduction by 2030 which will be planned and implemented over the years according to the SECAP monitoring results. These actions will be planned and implemented over the years with studies such as energy efficiency and renewable energy, innovation, technological improvements, renewals, system optimizations.

4.4.2. IMM 
Buildings 
Energy 
Efficiency Actions
The municipality's buildings will begin their energy efficiency journey by modernizing the largest and most energy-intensive buildings. 19 buildings have been identified and have undergone energy audits and are ready to start their modernization projects. 
The total investment required for the implementation of the planned measures in these 19 buildings is estimated to be around 3.7 million EUR. 
The modernization projects are expected to result in savings of around 
17.000 MWh/year or 1,1 million EUR per year at current energy prices. The simple payback period of investments is 
3 years. 
The remaining buildings of the municipality will be grouped according to their energy consumption in order to prioritize their modernization, starting with the most energy-intensive buildings. An accompanying building modernization plan for each year up to 
2030 must be developed, including the modernization of all remaining municipal buildings. 
Financial requirements for implementing the measures must be estimated and presented with each year's plan. The total investment for the implementation of the simple energy efficiency measures in the remaining municipal buildings is estimated to be around 1,75 million EUR. 
The modernization projects of remaining municipal buildings are expected to result in savings of around 
8.000 MWh/year or 0,5 million EUR per year at current energy prices. A simple payback period of investments estimated is 3 years.

[Page 169]
A program, consisting of installing solar 
PV plants and using alternative energy sources to generate around 50% of the municipality's current electricity demand by 2030, is planned in two phases.
In the first phase of the program, measures will be taken to ensure that 
18% of the municipality's current electricity demand is met through renewable sources by 2024. These measures will be primarily focused on the above-mentioned 19 buildings of the municipality. 
In order to generate 28,800 MWh of electricity per year, it is planned to install a several solar PV plant with a capacity of about 20 MWe. The total investment requirement for the installation of the solar PV plants is provisionally estimated at around 16 million EUR. The estimated simple payback period of investments is 4 to 5 years. The first phase is planned to be implemented in two steps, each including installation of a 10 MWe solar 
PV plant with 8 million EUR investment requirement.
The second phase will aim that 50% of the municipality's buildings total electricity demand will be generated by solar PV plants. To achieve this, the municipality plans to install an additional 60 MWe of solar PV plant. 
The total investment requirement for the installation of the solar PV plants is provisionally estimated at around 45 million EUR. Estimated simple payback period of investments is 4 to 5 years. 
This phase will be implemented in three steps, each including installation of a 20 
MWe solar PV plant with 15 million EUR investment requirement.
 
4.4.3. IMM 
Subsidiaries/Affiliate 
Companies Energy Efficiency Actions
Separate energy efficiency meetings were held with representatives of the aforementioned companies to analyze their activities, energy consumption data, and possible energy-saving measures. As part of the SECAP, short-term and medium-term objectives were established. To begin with, the national legislation, IMM's and its subsidiaries'/affiliates' obligations, and the 2023 energy reduction goals were identified. For the medium-term objectives, action plans for 2030 energy reduction were established to align with the 40% GHG emission reduction target, focusing solely on buildings and facilities. Furthermore, as part of IMM’s net zero carbon targets for 2050, an advanced renewable energy action plan aiming to achieve a minimum of 50% energy consumption of 2019 from İstanbul Metropolitan Municipality

[Page 170]
renewable sources by 2030 for the seven aforementioned subsidiaries and affiliates were developed.
In the IMM SECAP Report detailed and comprehensive studies were conducted on the seven largest energy-using and emission-emitting companies in order to achieve both the national goals in the short term and the SECAP 2030 targets in the medium term. Commonly, 
SECAP Reports provide general targets and action-mitigation plans for the city and municipality. IMM SECAP Report provides a unique approach to energy efficiency and emissions reduction that is tailored to the specific needs and targets of the municipality.
Total energy efficiency mitigation actions and renewable energy targets of 7 IMM affiliates/subsidiaries are provided in Table 25-Table 31. The detailed energy reduction targets for 
IMM buildings and facilities are listed in 
Annex 1.

4.4.4. Additional Measures
In order to reduce energy consumption and promote the use of renewable energy sources, additional measures will be implemented (Table 33). These measures will include training and awareness-raising activities. Training will be conducted to educate people on the importance of energy conservation and the use of renewable energy sources. The training will cover topics such as energy efficiency, renewable energy technologies, and energy conservation practices. Awareness raising activities will also be conducted to inform people about the benefits of using renewable energy sources and the importance of energy conservation. These activities will include public campaigns, workshops, and seminars. The implementation of these additional measures will help to reduce energy consumption and promote the use of renewable energy sources.
Energy literacy training will be provided to IMM personnel to ensure the effective and efficient use of energy in buildings and facilities. Through these trainings, personnel will gain an awareness of energy efficiency and management, enabling them to apply these learnings to their own homes and neighborhoods. As a result, they will be more conscious of energy conservation and sustainability.

MONITORING OF MITIGATION ACTIONS FOR BUILDINGS
Establishing a monitoring process is an important part of the implementation of the 
SECAP. This process allows for the measurement of progress towards the targets. It is essential to identify measurable and observable indicators that can be used to track progress and make timely decisions to ensure the implementation of measures and the achievement of results. These indicators must be easily understood and accounted for, and the responsible persons or institutions for the collection and presentation of data must be identified. Monitoring should include feedback from stakeholders, such as other local authorities, businesses, and citizens.

ENERGY ACCESS AND POVERTY 
ASSESSMENT
IMM is primarily making plans to switch and access renewable energy for its buildings and facilities. In 2022, PV roof applications with an installed capacity of 
3,3 MWe were carried out in 19 facilities. 
In this context, efforts will be made to facilitate the production of sustainable, accessible, and affordable electricity for all citizens with suitable roofs and residential, industrial and commercial facilities. 
Technical support and municipal approvals will be expedited to enable citizens to establish energy cooperatives and to promote community-oriented energy production. Technical project supervision and monitoring services will be provided to ensure that these initiatives are successful.
In 2020-2021 pandemic, IMM launched campaigns to pay the electricity and natural gas bills of citizens who were unable to pay their energy bills, particularly during the pandemic. Through these campaigns, tens of thousands of citizens' bills were successfully paid.
IMM is committed to tackling energy poverty, recognizing the connection between lack of access to affordable energy, poverty, inequality, and environmental issues. Inefficient building insulation and heating systems can lead to higher energy bills, creating a financial and health burden for many households. This can also cause health risks due to mold, cold, and low indoor air quality, further deepening the poverty of the most vulnerable population groups and widening the social inequality gap in cities. 
Additionally, inefficient buildings with high energy demands from fossil fuels contribute to higher greenhouse gas emissions than efficient dwellings. IMM is determined to address these issues and reduce energy poverty.
IMM is planning to join forces with 
TMMOB, Yıldız Technical University and İstanbul Technical University (İTÜ) and other universities to combat energy poverty in İstanbul. 
Through this partnership, university students will receive training from TMMOB to carry out simple energy audits in households that are struggling to pay their energy bills and to implement low-cost energy improvements. 
More than million households in İstanbul are having difficulty in paying their energy bills. IMM is keen on taking action to eradicate energy poverty. 
This partnership between the IMM and 
TMMOB, sectoral associations, 
Yıldız 
Technical University and İTÜ will be an important step towards achieving this goal.
Sustainable Energy and Climate Action Plan of İstanbul

[Page 199]
This project proposed in this context has main objectives: mapping energy-poor households, implementing low-cost energy-efficiency measures, and providing advice on how to reduce energy use. The 4th grade students in a university program will be engaged to help vulnerable households in İstanbul reduce their energy consumption. 
During the initial mapping and training phase, a group of hundred students, researchers, and university teachers will be trained by experts and TMMOB as well as sectoral associations to carry out social field research and energy audits. As energy poverty is not part of the engineering curriculum, IMM will choose vulnerable households to target and will invite them to participate in the project. Students will then conduct field visits to implement simple, low-cost energy efficiency interventions and gather data through surveys.
Over the course of five years, 1000 households will be visited and provided with low-cost energy-efficiency measures such as LED bulbs, draught-proofing of windows and doors, water-saving aerators, rooftop solar PV and timers for electric boilers. These measures, installed by students at no cost, will result in improved living conditions and long-term benefits.
By utilizing standard calculations, households can save up to 200 kg of CO2 and more than 1000 kWh of electricity and heat annually. As part of the program, students will advise households on the most cost-effective and energy-efficient measures based on a model for wall-retrofitting investments. This model demonstrates that wall-retrofitting investments with external thermal insulation are cost-effective for 30-50% of households (depending on heating systems and wall materials) with a payback period of 6-8 years or less, and other ancillary benefits such as improved human health due to better living conditions. 
This multi-stakeholder collaboration provides simple, low-cost energy-efficiency measures to energy-poor households. 
Furthermore, university students experience carrying out simple energy audits and implement field research methods and become agents for a fair energy transition. Table 36 provides an overview of the activity.
